class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;text-align:left">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of the United Kingdom">United href="/wiki/Interwar_Britain" title="Interwar Britain">Interwar period</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x931945" title="History of the United Ireland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Isle_of_Man#Modern_period" title="History of the Isle of Man">Late modern Mann</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_the_British_Isles" title="Template talk:History of the British Isles"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_the_British_Isles&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <span class="nowrap"><b>history of Scotland</b></span> is known to have begun by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period#Devensian_and_Midlandian_glaciation_(Britain_and_Ireland)" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> (in the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">paleolithic</a>), roughly 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">years ago</a>. <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Scotland" title="Prehistoric Scotland">Prehistoric Scotland</a> entered the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Neolithic Era">Neolithic Era</a> about 4000&#160;BC, the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> about 2000&#160;BC, and the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> around 700&#160;BC. Scotland\'s recorded history began with the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1st_century" title="1st century">1st&#160;century</a>, when the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a> reached as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a>. North of this was <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonia</a>, inhabited by the <i>Picti</i>, whose uprisings forced Rome\'s legions back to <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian\'s Wall</a>. As Rome finally <a href="/wiki/Roman_withdrawal_from_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman withdrawal from Britain">withdrew from Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> raiders called the <i><a href="/wiki/Scoti" title="Scoti">Scoti</a></i> began colonising Western Scotland and Wales.\n</p><p>The Gaelic kingdom of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> was founded on the west coast of Scotland in the 6th century. In the following century, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_Ireland" title="History of Christianity in Ireland">Irish</a> missionaries introduced the previously <a href="/wiki/Pictish_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Pictish religion">pagan Picts</a> to <a href="/wiki/Celtic_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic church">Celtic Christianity</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon England">England</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a>, the Pictish king <a href="/wiki/Nechtan_mac_Der-Ilei" title="Nechtan mac Der-Ilei">Nechtan</a> chose to abolish most Celtic practices in favour of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman rite</a>, restricting Gaelic influence on his kingdom and avoiding war with <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglian</a> <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of the 8th century, the <a href="/wiki/Viking_invasions_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking invasions of Scotland">Viking invasions</a> began, forcing the Picts and Gaels to cease their historic hostility to each other and to unite in the 9th century, forming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Kingdom of Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>The Kingdom of Scotland was united under the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>, whose members fought among each other during frequent disputed successions. The last Alpin king, <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_II_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm II of Scotland">Malcolm II</a>, died without issue in the early 11th century and the kingdom passed through his daughter\'s son to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Dunkeld" title="House of Dunkeld">House of Dunkeld</a> or Canmore. The last Dunkeld king, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>, died in 1286. He left only his infant granddaughter <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret, Maid of Norway</a> as heir, who died herself four years later. England, under <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I</a>, would take advantage of this questioned succession to launch a series of conquests, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a>, as Scotland passed back and forth between the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Balliol" title="House of Balliol">House of Balliol</a> and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Bruce" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Bruce">House of Bruce</a>. Scotland\'s ultimate victory confirmed Scotland as a fully independent and sovereign kingdom.\n</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">King David II</a> died without issue, his nephew <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II</a> established the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a>, which would rule Scotland uncontested for the next three centuries. <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James VI</a>, Stuart king of Scotland, also inherited the throne of England in 1603, and the Stuart kings and queens ruled both independent kingdoms until the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union,_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union, 1707">Act of Union</a> in 1707 merged the two kingdoms into a new state, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Ruling until 1714, <a href="/wiki/Anne,_Queen_of_Great_Britain" title="Anne, Queen of Great Britain">Queen Anne</a> was the last Stuart monarch. Since 1714, the succession of the <a href="/wiki/British_monarchs" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchs">British monarchs</a> of the houses of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hanover" title="House of Hanover">Hanover</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" title="House of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Saxe-Coburg and Gotha (Windsor)</a> has been due to their descent from <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James VI and I</a> of the House of Stuart.\n</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, Scotland became one of the commercial, intellectual and industrial powerhouses of Europe. Later, its <a href="/wiki/De-industrialization" class="mw-redirect" title="De-industrialization">industrial decline</a> following the Second World War was particularly acute. In recent decades Scotland has enjoyed something of a cultural and economic renaissance, fuelled in part by a resurgent <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> sector and the proceeds of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> and gas. Since the 1950s, nationalism has become a strong political topic, with serious debates on <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a>, and a referendum in 2014 about leaving the British Union.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" system</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Enlightenment"><span class="tocnumber">9.5</span> <span class="toctext">Enlightenment</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Beginnings_of_industrialisation"><span class="tocnumber">9.6</span> <span class="toctext">Beginnings of industrialisation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Religious_fragmentation"><span class="tocnumber">9.7</span> <span class="toctext">Religious fragmentation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">9.8</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Education"><span class="tocnumber">9.9</span> <span class="toctext">Education</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#19th_century"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">19th century</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Party_politics"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Party politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Industrial_expansion"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Industrial expansion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Public_health_and_welfare"><span class="tocnumber">10.3</span> <span class="toctext">Public health and welfare</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Intellectual_life"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Intellectual life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Decline_and_romanticism_of_the_Highlands"><span class="tocnumber">10.5</span> <span class="toctext">Decline and romanticism of the Highlands</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Rural_life"><span class="tocnumber">10.6</span> <span class="toctext">Rural life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Migration"><span class="tocnumber">10.7</span> <span class="toctext">Migration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-34"><a href="#Religious_schism_and_revival"><span class="tocnumber">10.8</span> <span class="toctext">Religious schism and revival</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-35"><a href="#Development_of_state_education"><span class="tocnumber">10.9</span> <span class="toctext">Development of state education</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Early_20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Early 20th century</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Fishing"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Fishing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Political_realignment"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Political realignment</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#First_World_War_(1914\xe2\x80\x941918)"><span class="tocnumber">11.3</span> <span class="toctext">First World War (1914\xe2\x80\x941918)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Economic_boom_and_stagnation"><span class="tocnumber">11.4</span> <span class="toctext">Economic boom and stagnation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Interwar_politics"><span class="tocnumber">11.5</span> <span class="toctext">Interwar politics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x9345"><span class="tocnumber">11.6</span> <span class="toctext">Second World War 1939\xe2\x80\x9345</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-43"><a href="#End_of_mass_migration"><span class="tocnumber">11.7</span> <span class="toctext">End of mass migration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Literary_renaissance"><span class="tocnumber">11.8</span> <span class="toctext">Literary renaissance</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Educational_reorganisation_and_retrenchment"><span class="tocnumber">11.9</span> <span class="toctext">Educational reorganisation and retrenchment</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-46"><a href="#Naval_role"><span class="tocnumber">11.10</span> <span class="toctext">Naval role</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Postwar"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Postwar</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-48"><a href="#Politics_and_devolution"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Politics and devolution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-49"><a href="#Economic_reorientation"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Economic reorientation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-50"><a href="#Religious_diversity_and_decline"><span class="tocnumber">12.3</span> <span class="toctext">Religious diversity and decline</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Educational_reforms"><span class="tocnumber">12.4</span> <span class="toctext">Educational reforms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#New_literature"><span class="tocnumber">12.5</span> <span class="toctext">New literature</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-53"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-54"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-55"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-56"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">15.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-57"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">15.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-58"><a href="#Surveys_and_reference_books"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Surveys and reference books</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-59"><a href="#Specialized_studies"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Specialized studies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-60"><a href="#Culture_and_religion"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Culture and religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-61"><a href="#Prehistory_and_archaeology"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Prehistory and archaeology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-62"><a href="#Medieval"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Medieval</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-63"><a href="#Early_modern"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Early modern</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-64"><a href="#Enlightenment,_18th_century"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Enlightenment, 18th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-65"><a href="#Union_and_Jacobites"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.8</span> <span class="toctext">Union and Jacobites</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-66"><a href="#Women"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.9</span> <span class="toctext">Women</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-67"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.10</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-68"><a href="#Primary_sources"><span class="tocnumber">15.2.11</span> <span class="toctext">Primary sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-69"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-history">Pre-history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Pre-history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_Scotland" title="Prehistoric Scotland">Prehistoric Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg/220px-Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg/330px-Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg/440px-Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Knapp_of_Howar_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The oldest standing house in Northern Europe is at <a href="/wiki/Knap_of_Howar" title="Knap of Howar">Knap of Howar</a>, dating from 3500&#160;BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>People lived in Scotland for at least 8,500 years before Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a>. At times during the last <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">interglacial period</a> (130,000\xe2\x80\x9370,000&#160;BC) Europe had a climate warmer than today\'s, and early humans may have made their way to Scotland, with the possible discovery of pre-<a href="/wiki/Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice Age">Ice Age</a> axes on <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> and mainland Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Glaciers then scoured their way across most of Britain, and only after the ice retreated did Scotland again become habitable, around 9600&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> hunter-gatherer encampments formed the first known settlements, and archaeologists have dated an encampment near <a href="/wiki/Biggar,_South_Lanarkshire" title="Biggar, South Lanarkshire">Biggar</a> to around 12000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Numerous other sites found around Scotland build up a picture of highly mobile boat-using people making tools from bone, stone and antlers.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The oldest house for which there is evidence in Britain is the oval structure of wooden posts found at <a href="/wiki/South_Queensferry" title="South Queensferry">South Queensferry</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a>, dating from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> period, about 8240&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The earliest stone structures are probably the three hearths found at <a href="/wiki/Jura,_Scotland" title="Jura, Scotland">Jura</a>, dated to about 6000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> farming brought permanent settlements. Evidence of these includes the well-preserved stone house at <a href="/wiki/Knap_of_Howar" title="Knap of Howar">Knap of Howar</a> on <a href="/wiki/Papa_Westray" title="Papa Westray">Papa Westray</a>, dating from around 3500&#160;BC<sup id="cite_ref-Maxwell2005p19_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell2005p19-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and the village of similar houses at <a href="/wiki/Skara_Brae" title="Skara Brae">Skara Brae</a> on West <a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Orkney" title="Mainland, Orkney">Mainland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> from about 500 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-Pryor2003pp98-104&amp;246-50_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pryor2003pp98-104&amp;246-50-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The settlers introduced <a href="/wiki/Chambered_cairn" title="Chambered cairn">chambered cairn</a> tombs from around 3500&#160;BC, as at <a href="/wiki/Maeshowe" title="Maeshowe">Maeshowe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Fry&amp;Fry1992p7_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fry&amp;Fry1992p7-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> and from about 3000&#160;BC the many standing stones and circles such as those at <a href="/wiki/Standing_Stones_of_Stenness" title="Standing Stones of Stenness">Stenness</a> on the mainland of Orkney, which date from about 3100&#160;BC, of four stones, the tallest of which is 16 feet (5&#160;m) in height.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> These were part of a pattern that developed in many regions across Europe at about the same time.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The creation of cairns and Megalithic monuments continued into the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, which began in Scotland about 2000&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As elsewhere in Europe, <a href="/wiki/Hill_forts" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill forts">hill forts</a> were first introduced in this period, including the occupation of <a href="/wiki/Eildon_Hill" title="Eildon Hill">Eildon Hill</a> near Melrose in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Borders" title="Scottish Borders">Scottish Borders</a>, from around 1000&#160;BC, which accommodated several hundred houses on a fortified hilltop.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> From the <a href="/wiki/Early_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Bronze Age">Early</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Bronze_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Bronze Age">Middle Bronze Age</a> there is evidence of cellular round houses of stone, as at <a href="/wiki/Jarlshof" title="Jarlshof">Jarlshof</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumburgh,_Shetland" title="Sumburgh, Shetland">Sumburgh</a> on Shetland.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunliffe2004p60_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe2004p60-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> There is also evidence of the occupation of <a href="/wiki/Crannog" title="Crannog">crannogs</a>, roundhouses partially or entirely built on artificial islands, usually in lakes, rivers and estuarine waters.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, from the seventh century&#160;BC, cellular houses began to be replaced on the northern isles by simple <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_roundhouse" title="Atlantic roundhouse">Atlantic roundhouses</a>, substantial circular buildings with a dry stone construction. From about 400&#160;BC, more complex Atlantic roundhouses began to be built, as at Howe, Orkney and <a href="/wiki/Crosskirk_Broch" title="Crosskirk Broch">Crosskirk</a>, Caithness.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunliffe2004p325_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe2004p325-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The most massive constructions that date from this era are the circular <a href="/wiki/Broch" title="Broch">broch</a> towers, probably dating from about 200&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-Cunliffe2004p325_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cunliffe2004p325-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> This period also saw the first <a href="/wiki/Wheelhouse_(archaeology)" title="Wheelhouse (archaeology)">wheelhouses</a>, a roundhouse with a characteristic outer wall, within which was a circle of stone piers (bearing a resemblance to the spokes of a wheel), but these would flourish most in the era of Roman occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner1999p81_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner1999p81-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> There is evidence for about 1,000 Iron Age <a href="/wiki/List_of_hill_forts_in_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="List of hill forts in Scotland">hill forts in Scotland</a>, most located below the Clyde-Forth line,<sup id="cite_ref-Lepage2010pp25-31_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lepage2010pp25-31-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> which have suggested to some archaeologists the emergence of a society of petty rulers and warrior elites recognisable from Roman accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_invasion">Roman invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Roman invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_during_the_Roman_Empire" title="Scotland during the Roman Empire">Scotland during the Roman Empire</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG/170px-Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG/255px-Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG/340px-Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1904" data-file-height="2433" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bridgeness_slab_detail.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a> cavalryman trampling conquered <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a>, on a tablet found at <a href="/wiki/Bo%27ness" title="Bo&#39;ness">Bo\'ness</a> dated to c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;142</span> and now in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Scotland" title="National Museum of Scotland">National Museum of Scotland</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The surviving pre-Roman accounts of Scotland originated with the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">Pytheas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marseille" title="Marseille">Massalia</a>, who may have circumnavigated the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> of <a href="/wiki/Albion" title="Albion">Albion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ierne_(placename)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ierne (placename)">Ierne</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LSJBrettanike_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSJBrettanike-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> sometime around 325&#160;BC. The most northerly point of Britain was called <i>Orcas</i> (Orkney).<sup id="cite_ref-Diodorus(5.21.4)_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diodorus(5.21.4)-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> By the time of <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, who died in AD&#160;79, Roman knowledge of the geography of Scotland had extended to the <i>Hebudes</i> (<a href="/wiki/The_Hebrides" class="mw-redirect" title="The Hebrides">The Hebrides</a>), <i>Dumna</i> (probably the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_Forest" title="Caledonian Forest">Caledonian Forest</a> and the people of the <a href="/wiki/Caledonians" title="Caledonians">Caledonii</a>, from whom the Romans named the region north of their control <a href="/wiki/Caledonia" title="Caledonia">Caledonia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>, possibly drawing on earlier sources of information as well as more contemporary accounts from the <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Agricolan</a> invasion, identified 18 tribes in Scotland<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_Geography" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemy&#39;s Geography">his <i>Geography</i></a>, but many of the names are obscure and the geography becomes less reliable in the north and west, suggesting early Roman knowledge of these areas was confined to observations from the sea.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_invasion_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman invasion of Britain">Roman invasion of Britain</a> began in earnest in AD&#160;43, leading to the establishment of the Roman province of <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a> in the south. By the year 71, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Roman_governors_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Roman governors of Britain">Roman governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Petillius_Cerialis" title="Quintus Petillius Cerialis">Quintus Petillius Cerialis</a> had launched an invasion of what is now Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In the year 78, <a href="/wiki/Gnaeus_Julius_Agricola" title="Gnaeus Julius Agricola">Gnaeus Julius Agricola</a> arrived in Britain to take up his appointment as the new governor and began a series of major incursions. He is said to have pushed his armies to the estuary of the "River Taus" (usually assumed to be the <a href="/wiki/River_Tay" title="River Tay">River Tay</a>) and established forts there, including a legionary fortress at <a href="/wiki/Inchtuthil" title="Inchtuthil">Inchtuthil</a>. After his victory over the northern tribes at <a href="/wiki/Mons_Graupius" class="mw-redirect" title="Mons Graupius">Mons Graupius</a> in 84, a series of forts and towers were established along the <a href="/wiki/Gask_Ridge" title="Gask Ridge">Gask Ridge</a>, which marked the boundary between the Lowland and Highland zones, probably forming the first Roman <i>limes</i> or frontier in Scotland. Agricola\'s successors were unable or unwilling to further subdue the far north.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> By the year 87, the occupation was limited to the Southern Uplands and by the end of the first century the northern limit of Roman expansion was a line drawn between the <a href="/wiki/River_Tyne" title="River Tyne">Tyne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Solway_Firth" title="Solway Firth">Solway Firth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The Romans eventually withdrew to a line in what is now northern England, building the fortification known as <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian&#39;s Wall">Hadrian\'s Wall</a> from coast to coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Antoninewall_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antoninewall-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Around 141, the Romans undertook a reoccupation of southern Scotland, moving up to construct a new <i><a href="/wiki/Limes_(Roman)" class="mw-redirect" title="Limes (Roman)">limes</a></i> between the Firth of Forth and the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>, which became the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a>. The largest Roman construction inside Scotland, it is a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sward" class="extiw" title="wikt:sward">sward</a>-covered wall made of <a href="/wiki/Sod" title="Sod">turf</a> around 20 feet (6&#160;m) high, with nineteen forts. It extended for 37 miles (60&#160;km). Having taken twelve years to build, the wall was overrun and abandoned soon after 160.<sup id="cite_ref-Antoninewall_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antoninewall-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The Romans retreated to the line of Hadrian\'s Wall.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Roman troops penetrated far into the north of modern Scotland several more times, with at least four major campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The most notable invasion was in 209 when the emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> led a major force north.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogan_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogan-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> After the death of Severus in 210 they withdrew south to Hadrian\'s Wall, which would be Roman frontier until it collapsed in the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> By the close of the Roman occupation of southern and central Britain in the 5th century, the <a href="/wiki/Picts" title="Picts">Picts</a> had emerged as the dominant force in northern Scotland, with the various <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a> tribes the Romans had first encountered there occupying the southern half of the country. Roman influence on Scottish culture and history was not enduring.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Roman_Scotland">Post-Roman Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-Roman Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Early_Middle_Ages" title="Scotland in the Early Middle Ages">Scotland in the Early Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg/170px-Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg" width="170" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg/255px-Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/24/Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="302" data-file-height="324" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pictish_stone_strathpeffer_eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clach_an_Tiompain" title="Clach an Tiompain">Clach an Tiompain</a>, a Pictish symbol stone in <a href="/wiki/Strathpeffer" title="Strathpeffer">Strathpeffer</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the centuries after the departure of the Romans from Britain, there were four groups within the borders of what is now Scotland. In the east were the Picts, with kingdoms between the river Forth and Shetland. In the late 6th century the dominant force was the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Fortriu" title="Fortriu">Fortriu</a>, whose lands were centred on <a href="/wiki/Strathearn" title="Strathearn">Strathearn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menteith" title="Menteith">Menteith</a> and who raided along the eastern coast into modern England.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the west were the Gaelic (<a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a>)-speaking people of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1l_Riata" title="D\xc3\xa1l Riata">D\xc3\xa1l Riata</a> with their royal fortress at <a href="/wiki/Dunadd" title="Dunadd">Dunadd</a> in Argyll, with close links with the island of Ireland, from whom comes the name Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-Smyth1989pp43-6_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smyth1989pp43-6-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In the south was the British (Brythonic) <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Strathclyde" title="Kingdom of Strathclyde">Kingdom of Strathclyde</a>, descendants of the peoples of the Roman influenced kingdoms of "<a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Hen Ogledd</a>" (Old north), often named Alt Clut, the Brythonic name for their capital at <a href="/wiki/Dumbarton_Rock" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumbarton Rock">Dumbarton Rock</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Finally, there were the English or "Angles", Germanic invaders who had overrun much of southern Britain and held the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bernicia" title="Bernicia">Bernicia</a>, in the south-east.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The first English king in the historical record is <a href="/wiki/Ida_of_Bernicia" title="Ida of Bernicia">Ida</a>, who is said to have obtained the throne and the kingdom about 547.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Ida\xe2\x80\x99s grandson, \xc3\x86thelfrith, united his kingdom with <a href="/wiki/Deira_(kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Deira (kingdom)">Deira</a> to the south to form Northumbria around the year 604. There were changes of dynasty, and the kingdom was divided, but it was re-united under \xc3\x86thelfrith\'s son <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a> (r. 634-42).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland was largely converted to Christianity by Irish-Scots missions associated with figures such as <a href="/wiki/St_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="St Columba">St Columba</a>, from the fifth to the seventh centuries. These missions tended to found <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monastic</a> institutions and collegiate churches that served large areas.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Partly as a result of these factors, some scholars have identified a distinctive form of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a>, in which <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbots</a> were more significant than <a href="/wiki/Bishop" title="Bishop">bishops</a>, attitudes to <a href="/wiki/Clerical_celibacy" title="Clerical celibacy">clerical celibacy</a> were more relaxed and there was some significant differences in practice with Roman Christianity, particularly the form of <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a> and the method of <a href="/wiki/Computus" title="Computus">calculating Easter</a>, although most of these issues had been resolved by the mid-7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans1985_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans1985-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba">Rise of the Kingdom of Alba</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rise of the Kingdom of Alba">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Origins_of_the_Kingdom_of_Alba" title="Origins of the Kingdom of Alba">Origins of the Kingdom of Alba</a></div>\n<p>Conversion to Christianity may have speeded a long term process of gaelicisation of the Pictish kingdoms, which adopted Gaelic language and customs. There was also a merger of the Gaelic and Pictish crowns, although historians debate whether it was a Pictish takeover of D\xc3\xa1l Riata, or the other way around. This culminated in the rise of <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kenneth I of Scotland">C\xc3\xadnaed mac Ailp\xc3\xadn</a> (Kenneth MacAlpin) in the 840s, which brought to power the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Alpin" title="House of Alpin">House of Alpin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yorke2006p54_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yorke2006p54-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> In 867 AD the Vikings seized the southern half of Northumbria, forming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of York">Kingdom of York</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> three years later they stormed the Britons\xe2\x80\x99 fortress of Dumbarton<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> and subsequently conquered much of England except for a reduced Kingdom of Wessex,<sup id="cite_ref-Rollason2003p212_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rollason2003p212-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> leaving the new combined Pictish and Gaelic kingdom almost encircled.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> When he died as king of the combined kingdom in 900, <a href="/wiki/Donald_II_of_Scotland" title="Donald II of Scotland">Domnall II</a> (Donald II) was the first man to be called <i>r\xc3\xad Alban</i> (i.e. <i>King of Alba</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The term Scotia was increasingly used to describe the kingdom between North of the Forth and Clyde and eventually the entire area controlled by its kings was referred to as Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997p22_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997p22-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map,_c.1250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/220px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/330px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg/440px-Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map%2C_c.1250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="522" data-file-height="399" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scotland_from_the_Matthew_Paris_map,_c.1250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scotland from the <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Paris" title="Matthew Paris">Matthew Paris</a> map, c. 1250</div></div></div>\n<p>The long reign (900\xe2\x80\x93942/3) of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_II_of_Scotland" title="Constantine II of Scotland">Causant\xc3\xadn</a> (Constantine II) is often regarded as the key to formation of the Kingdom of Alba. He was later credited with bringing Scottish Christianity into conformity with the Catholic Church. After fighting many battles, his defeat at <a href="/wiki/Brunanburh" class="mw-redirect" title="Brunanburh">Brunanburh</a> was followed by his retirement as a <a href="/wiki/Culdee" class="mw-redirect" title="Culdee">Culdee</a> monk at St. Andrews.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The period between the accession of his successor <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_I_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm I of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I</a> (Malcolm I) and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim mac Cin\xc3\xa1eda (Malcolm II) was marked by good relations with the <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of England">rulers of England</a>, intense internal dynastic disunity and relatively successful expansionary policies. In 945, M\xc3\xa1el Coluim I annexed Strathclyde as part of a deal with King <a href="/wiki/Edmund_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Edmund I of England">Edmund of England</a>, where the kings of Alba had probably exercised some authority since the later 9th century,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> an event offset somewhat by loss of control in Moray. The reign of King <a href="/wiki/Duncan_I_of_Scotland" title="Duncan I of Scotland">Donnchad I</a> (Duncan I) from 1034 was marred by failed military adventures, and he was defeated and killed by MacBeth, the <a href="/wiki/Mormaer_of_Moray" title="Mormaer of Moray">Mormaer of Moray</a>, who became king in 1040.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> MacBeth ruled for seventeen years before he was overthrown by <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_III_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm III of Scotland">M\xc3\xa1el Coluim</a>, the son of Donnchad, who some months later defeated MacBeth\'s <a href="/wiki/Stepfamily" title="Stepfamily">step-son</a> and successor Lulach to become king M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III (Malcolm III).<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie43_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie43-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was M\xc3\xa1el Coluim III, who acquired the nickname "Canmore" (<i>Cenn M\xc3\xb3r</i>, "Great Chief"), which he passed to his successors and who did most to create the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Dunkeld" title="House of Dunkeld">Dunkeld dynasty</a> that ruled Scotland for the following two centuries. Particularly important was his second marriage to the Anglo-Hungarian princess <a href="/wiki/Saint_Margaret_of_Scotland" title="Saint Margaret of Scotland">Margaret</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> This marriage, and raids on northern England, prompted <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of England">William the Conqueror</a> to invade and M\xc3\xa1el Coluim submitted to his authority, opening up Scotland to later claims of sovereignty by English kings.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> When Malcolm died in 1093, his brother <a href="/wiki/Donald_III_of_Scotland" title="Donald III of Scotland">Domnall III</a> (Donald III) succeeded him. However, <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II of England</a> backed M\xc3\xa1el Coluim\'s son by his first marriage, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Duncan II">Donnchad</a>, as a pretender to the throne and he seized power. His murder within a few months saw Domnall restored with one of M\xc3\xa1el Coluim sons by his second marriage, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Scotland" title="Edmund of Scotland">Edmund</a>, as his heir. The two ruled Scotland until two of Edmund\'s younger brothers returned from exile in England, again with English military backing. Victorious, <a href="/wiki/Edgar_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Edgar of Scotland">Edgar</a>, the oldest of the three, became king in 1097.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp23-4_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp23-4-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Shortly afterwards Edgar and the King of Norway, <a href="/wiki/Magnus_III_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus III of Norway">Magnus Bare Legs</a> concluded a treaty recognising Norwegian authority over the Western Isles. In practice Norse control of the Isles was loose, with local chiefs enjoying a high degree of independence. He was succeeded by his brother <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Scotland" title="Alexander I of Scotland">Alexander</a>, who reigned 1107\xe2\x80\x9324.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III,Llywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg/220px-Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg/330px-Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg/440px-Alexander_III%2CLlywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1010" data-file-height="790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_III,Llywelyn_ab_Gruffydd_with_Edward_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>King Alexander III of Scotland on the left with Llywelyn, Prince of Wales on the right as guests to King Edward I of England at the sitting of an English parliament.</div></div></div>\n<p>When Alexander died in 1124, the crown passed to Margaret\'s fourth son <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>, who had spent most of his life as a Norman French baron in England. His reign saw what has been characterised as a "<a href="/wiki/Davidian_Revolution" title="Davidian Revolution">Davidian Revolution</a>", by which native institutions and personnel were replaced by English and French ones, underpinning the development of later Medieval Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrow1992pp9-11_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrow1992pp9-11-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Members of the Anglo-Norman nobility took up places in the Scottish aristocracy and he introduced a system of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> land tenure, which produced <a href="/wiki/Knight_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Knight service">knight service</a>, castles and an available body of heavily armed cavalry. He created an Anglo-Norman style of court, introduced the office of <a href="/wiki/Justicar" class="mw-redirect" title="Justicar">justicar</a> to oversee justice, and local offices of <a href="/wiki/Sheriffs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheriffs">sheriffs</a> to administer localities. He established the first <a href="/wiki/Royal_burgh" title="Royal burgh">royal burghs</a> in Scotland, granting rights to particular settlements, which led to the development of the first true Scottish towns and helped facilitate economic development as did the introduction of the first recorded Scottish coinage. He continued a process begun by his mother and brothers helping to establish foundations that brought reform to Scottish monasticism based on those at <a href="/wiki/Cluny_Abbey" title="Cluny Abbey">Cluny</a> and he played a part in organising diocese on lines closer to those in the rest of Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp29-37_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp29-37-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>These reforms were pursued under his successors and grandchildren <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_IV_of_Scotland" title="Malcolm IV of Scotland">Malcolm IV of Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_I_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="William I of Scotland">William I</a>, with the crown now passing down the main line of descent through primogeniture, leading to the first of a series of minorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Webster1997pp23-4_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Webster1997pp23-4-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The benefits of greater authority were reaped by William\'s son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Scotland" title="Alexander II of Scotland">Alexander II</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Alexander_III_of_Scotland" title="Alexander III of Scotland">Alexander III</a>, who pursued a policy of peace with England to expand their authority in the Highlands and Islands. By the reign of Alexander III, the Scots were in a position to annex the remainder of the western seaboard, which they did following <a href="/wiki/Haakon_IV_of_Norway" title="Haakon IV of Norway">Haakon Haakonarson</a>\'s ill-fated invasion and the stalemate of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Largs" title="Battle of Largs">Battle of Largs</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Perth" title="Treaty of Perth">Treaty of Perth</a> in 1266.<sup id="cite_ref-Macquarrie2004p153_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macquarrie2004p153-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Wars_of_Independence">The Wars of Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Wars of Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Scottish_Independence" title="Wars of Scottish Independence">Wars of Scottish Independence</a></div>\n<p>The death of king Alexander III in 1286, and the death of his granddaughter and heir <a href="/wiki/Margaret,_Maid_of_Norway" title="Margaret, Maid of Norway">Margaret, Maid of Norway</a> in 1290, left 14 rivals for succession. To prevent civil war the Scottish magnates asked <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a> to arbitrate, for which he extracted legal recognition that the realm of Scotland was held as a feudal dependency to the throne of England before choosing <a href="/wiki/John_Balliol" title="John Balliol">John Balliol</a>, the man with the strongest claim, who became king in 1292.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Bruce,_5th_Lord_of_Annandale" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Bruce, 5th Lord of Annandale">Robert Bruce, 5th Lord of Annandale</a>, the next strongest claimant, accepted this outcome with reluctance. Over the next few years Edward I used the concessions he had gained to systematically undermine both the authority of King John and the independence of Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In 1295, John, on the urgings of his chief councillors, entered into an alliance with France, known as the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_I_of_England,_Malleus_Scotorum_(%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27).tiff" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff/lossy-page1-170px-King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff/lossy-page1-255px-King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff/lossy-page1-340px-King_Edward_I_of_England%2C_Malleus_Scotorum_%28%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27%29.tiff.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1006" data-file-height="1345" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Edward_I_of_England,_Malleus_Scotorum_(%27Hammer_of_the_Scots%27).tiff" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Edward I of England, \'Hammer Of The Scots\', depicted on a late-15thC woodcut.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1296, Edward invaded Scotland, deposing King John. The following year <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_de_Moray" class="mw-redirect" title="Andrew de Moray">Andrew de Moray</a> raised forces to resist the occupation and under their joint leadership an English army was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>. For a short time Wallace ruled Scotland in the name of John Balliol as Guardian of the realm. Edward came north in person and defeated Wallace at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Falkirk" title="Battle of Falkirk">Battle of Falkirk</a> in 1298.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Wallace escaped but probably resigned as Guardian of Scotland. In 1305, he fell into the hands of the English, who executed him for treason despite the fact that he owed no allegiance to England.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rivals <a href="/wiki/John_III_Comyn,_Lord_of_Badenoch" title="John III Comyn, Lord of Badenoch">John Comyn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_the_Bruce" title="Robert the Bruce">Robert the Bruce</a>, grandson of the claimant, were appointed as joint guardians in his place.<sup id="cite_ref-Ross1999_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ross1999-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> On 10 February 1306, Bruce participated in the murder of Comyn, at Greyfriars Kirk in <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Less than seven weeks later, on 25 March, Bruce was crowned as King. However, Edward\'s forces overran the country after defeating Bruce\'s small army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Methven" title="Battle of Methven">Battle of Methven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Despite the excommunication of Bruce and his followers by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Pope Clement V</a>, his support slowly strengthened; and by 1314 with the help of leading nobles such as Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Douglas,_Lord_of_Douglas" title="James Douglas, Lord of Douglas">James Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Randolph,_1st_Earl_of_Moray" title="Thomas Randolph, 1st Earl of Moray">Thomas Randolph</a> only the castles at Bothwell and Stirling remained under English control.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Edward I had died in 1307. His heir <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> moved an army north to break the siege of <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a> and reassert control. Robert defeated that army at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bannockburn" title="Battle of Bannockburn">Battle of Bannockburn</a> in 1314, securing <i>de facto</i> independence.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 1320, the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Arbroath" title="Declaration of Arbroath">Declaration of Arbroath</a>, a remonstrance to the Pope from the nobles of Scotland, helped convince <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a> to overturn the earlier excommunication and nullify the various acts of submission by Scottish kings to English ones so that Scotland\'s sovereignty could be recognised by the major European dynasties. The Declaration has also been seen as one of the most important documents in the development of a Scottish national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nIn 1326, what may have been the first full <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> met. The parliament had evolved from an earlier council of nobility and clergy, the <i>colloquium</i>, constituted around 1235, but perhaps in 1326 representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a> \xe2\x80\x94 the burgh commissioners \xe2\x80\x94 joined them to form the <a href="/wiki/Three_Estates" class="mw-redirect" title="Three Estates">Three Estates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In 1328, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh%E2%80%93Northampton" title="Treaty of Edinburgh\xe2\x80\x93Northampton">Treaty of Edinburgh\xe2\x80\x93Northampton</a> acknowledging <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a> under the rule of Robert the Bruce.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> However, four years after Robert\'s death in 1329, England once more invaded on the pretext of restoring <a href="/wiki/Edward_Balliol" title="Edward Balliol">Edward Balliol</a>, son of John Balliol, to the Scottish throne, thus starting the Second War of Independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Despite victories at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dupplin_Moor" title="Battle of Dupplin Moor">Dupplin Moor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Halidon_Hill" title="Battle of Halidon Hill">Halidon Hill</a>, in the face of tough Scottish resistance led by <a href="/wiki/Sir_Andrew_Murray" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Andrew Murray">Sir Andrew Murray</a>, the son of Wallace\'s comrade in arms, successive attempts to secure Balliol on the throne failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Edward III lost interest in the fate of his prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9 after the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a> with France.<sup id="cite_ref-Keen2003_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keen2003-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> In 1341, <a href="/wiki/David_II_of_Scotland" title="David II of Scotland">David II</a>, King Robert\'s son and heir, was able to return from temporary exile in France. Balliol finally resigned his claim to the throne to Edward in 1356, before retiring to Yorkshire, where he died in 1364.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Stewarts">The Stewarts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Stewarts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/House_of_Stuart" title="House of Stuart">House of Stuart</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Berlinghieri,_Geographia,_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo,_firenze_1482,_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg/300px-Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg/450px-Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg/600px-Francesco_Berlinghieri%2C_Geographia%2C_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo%2C_firenze_1482%2C_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Francesco_Berlinghieri,_Geographia,_incunabolo_per_niccol%C3%B2_di_lorenzo,_firenze_1482,_09_isole_britanniche_03_scozia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Highlands in 1482</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg/170px-Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg/255px-Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg/340px-Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1403" data-file-height="1755" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Armorial_depiction_of_the_King_of_Scots.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Heraldic depiction of the King of Scots from a 15th-century French armorial</div></div></div>\n<p>After David II\'s death, <a href="/wiki/Robert_II_of_Scotland" title="Robert II of Scotland">Robert II</a>, the first of the Stewart kings, came to the throne in 1371. He was followed in 1390 by his ailing son John, who took the <a href="/wiki/Regnal_name" title="Regnal name">regnal name</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_III_of_Scotland" title="Robert III of Scotland">Robert III</a>. During Robert III\'s reign (1390\xe2\x80\x931406), actual power rested largely in the hands of his brother, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany">Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> After the suspicious death (possibly on the orders of the Duke of Albany) of his elder son, David, Duke of Rothesay in 1402, Robert, fearful for the safety of his younger son, the future <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_Scotland" title="James I of Scotland">James I</a>, sent him to France in 1406. However, the English captured him en route and he spent the next 18 years as a prisoner held for ransom. As a result, after the death of Robert III, regents ruled Scotland: first, the Duke of Albany; and later his son <a href="/wiki/Murdoch_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Murdoch Stewart, Duke of Albany">Murdoch</a>. When Scotland finally paid the ransom in 1424, James, aged 32, returned with his English bride determined to assert this authority.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>  Several of the Albany family were executed; but he succeeded in centralising control in the hands of the crown, at the cost of increasing unpopularity, and was assassinated in 1437. His son <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_Scotland" title="James II of Scotland">James II</a> (reigned 1437\xe2\x80\x931460), when he came of age in 1449, continued his father\'s policy of weakening the great noble families, most notably taking on the powerful <a href="/wiki/House_of_Douglas" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Douglas">Black Douglas family</a> that had come to prominence at the time of the Bruce.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1468, the last significant acquisition of Scottish territory occurred when <a href="/wiki/James_III_of_Scotland" title="James III of Scotland">James III</a> was engaged to <a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Denmark,_Queen_of_Scotland" title="Margaret of Denmark, Queen of Scotland">Margaret of Denmark</a>, receiving the <a href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a> in payment of her dowry.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Berwick_upon_Tweed" class="mw-redirect" title="Berwick upon Tweed">Berwick upon Tweed</a> was <a href="/wiki/Capture_of_Berwick_(1482)" class="mw-redirect" title="Capture of Berwick (1482)">captured by England in 1482</a>. With the death of James III in 1488 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sauchieburn" title="Battle of Sauchieburn">Battle of Sauchieburn</a>, his successor <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">James IV</a> successfully ended the quasi-independent rule of the <a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Isles" title="Lord of the Isles">Lord of the Isles</a>, bringing the Western Isles under effective Royal control for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-Rigby2003_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rigby2003-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In 1503, he married <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret Tudor</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a>, thus laying the foundation for the 17th century <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland advanced markedly in educational terms during the 15th century with the founding of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> in 1413, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a> in 1450 and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> in 1495, and with the passing of the <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1496" title="Education Act 1496">Education Act 1496</a>, which decreed that all sons of barons and freeholders of substance should attend grammar schools.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> James IV\'s reign is often considered to have seen a flowering of Scottish culture under the influence of the European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg/220px-View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg/330px-View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg/440px-View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:View_of_Edinburgh_from_the_Palace_block_of_Edinburgh_Castle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View from the royal apartments of the Stewart monarchs, Edinburgh Castle.</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1512, the Auld Alliance was renewed and under its terms, when the French were attacked by the English under <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII</a>, James IV invaded England in support. The invasion was stopped decisively at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Flodden_Field" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Flodden Field">Battle of Flodden Field</a> during which the King, many of his nobles, and a large number of ordinary troops were killed, commemorated by the song <i><a href="/wiki/Flowers_of_the_Forest" title="Flowers of the Forest">Flowers of the Forest</a></i>. Once again Scotland\'s government lay in the hands of regents in the name of the infant <a href="/wiki/James_V_of_Scotland" title="James V of Scotland">James V</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>James V finally managed to escape from the custody of the regents in 1528. He continued his father\'s policy of subduing the rebellious <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a>, Western and Northern isles and the troublesome borders.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls1999pp82-4_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls1999pp82-4-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> He also continued the French alliance, marrying first the French noblewoman <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_of_Valois" title="Madeleine of Valois">Madeleine of Valois</a> and then after her death <a href="/wiki/Marie_of_Guise" class="mw-redirect" title="Marie of Guise">Marie of Guise</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls1999pp82-4_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls1999pp82-4-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> James V\'s domestic and foreign policy successes were overshadowed by another disastrous campaign against England that led to defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Solway_Moss" title="Battle of Solway Moss">Battle of Solway Moss</a> (1542).<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls1999pp82-4_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls1999pp82-4-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> James died a short time later, a demise blamed by contemporaries on "a broken heart". The day before his death, he was brought news of the birth of an heir: a daughter, who would become <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Nicholls1999p87_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholls1999p87-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once again, Scotland was in the hands of a regent. Within two years, the <a href="/wiki/Rough_Wooing" title="Rough Wooing">Rough Wooing</a> began, Henry VIII\'s military attempt to force a marriage between Mary and his son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward</a>. This took the form of border skirmishing and several English campaigns into Scotland. In 1547, after the death of Henry VIII, forces under the English regent <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset</a> were victorious at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Pinkie_Cleugh" title="Battle of Pinkie Cleugh">Battle of Pinkie Cleugh</a>, the climax of the Rough Wooing, and followed up by the occupation of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Haddington" title="Siege of Haddington">Haddington</a>. Mary was then sent to France at the age of five, as the intended bride of the heir to the French throne. Her mother, Marie de Guise, stayed in Scotland to look after the interests of Mary \xe2\x80\x94 and of France \xe2\x80\x94 although the <a href="/wiki/James_Hamilton,_2nd_Earl_of_Arran" class="mw-redirect" title="James Hamilton, 2nd Earl of Arran">Earl of Arran</a> acted officially as regent.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Guise responded by calling on French troops, who helped stiffen resistance to the English occupation. By 1550, after a change of regent in England, the English withdrew from Scotland completely.\n</p><p>From 1554, Marie de Guise, took over the regency, and continued to advance French interests in Scotland. French cultural influence resulted in a large influx of French vocabulary into <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>. But anti-French sentiment also grew, particularly among <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a>, who saw the English as their natural allies. In 1560, Marie de Guise died, and soon after the Auld Alliance also ended, with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh" title="Treaty of Edinburgh">Treaty of Edinburgh</a>, which provided for the removal of French and English troops from Scotland. The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Scottish Reformation</a> took place only days later when the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation_Parliament" title="Scottish Reformation Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> abolished the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> religion and outlawed the <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">Mass</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg/220px-The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg/330px-The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg/440px-The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_murder_of_David_Rizzio_from_the_Duke_of_Buccleuch_statue.jpg"
