class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of <a href="/wiki/David_Rizzio" title="David Rizzio">David Rizzio</a>\'s murder in 1566</div></div></div>\n<p>Meanwhile, Queen Mary had been raised as a Catholic in France, and married to the <a href="/wiki/Dauphin_of_France" title="Dauphin of France">Dauphin</a>, who became king as <a href="/wiki/Francis_II_of_France" title="Francis II of France">Francis II</a> in 1559, making her queen consort of France.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> When Francis died in 1560, Mary, now 19, returned to Scotland to take up the government. Despite her private religion, she did not attempt to re-impose Catholicism on her largely Protestant subjects, thus angering the chief Catholic nobles. Her six-year personal reign was marred by a series of crises, largely caused by the intrigues and rivalries of the leading nobles. The murder of her secretary, <a href="/wiki/David_Riccio" class="mw-redirect" title="David Riccio">David Riccio</a>, was followed by that of her unpopular second husband <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Lord_Darnley" title="Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley">Lord Darnley</a>, and her abduction by and marriage to the <a href="/wiki/James_Hepburn,_4th_Earl_of_Bothwell" title="James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell">Earl of Bothwell</a>, who was implicated in Darnley\'s murder.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Mary and Bothwell confronted the lords at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carberry_Hill" title="Battle of Carberry Hill">Carberry Hill</a> and after their forces melted away, he fled and she was captured by Bothwell\'s rivals. Mary was imprisoned in <a href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_Castle" title="Loch Leven Castle">Loch Leven Castle</a>, and in July 1567, was forced to abdicate in favour of her infant son <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James VI</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Mary eventually escaped and attempted to regain the throne by force. After her defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Langside" title="Battle of Langside">Battle of Langside</a> in 1568, she took refuge in England, leaving her young son in the hands of regents. In Scotland the regents fought a <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_Castle#16th_century_and_the_Lang_Siege" title="Edinburgh Castle">civil war</a> on behalf of James VI against his mother\'s supporters. In England, Mary became a focal point for Catholic conspirators and was eventually tried for treason and executed on the orders of her kinswoman Elizabeth I.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_Reformation">Protestant Reformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Protestant Reformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Scottish Reformation</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Knoxbezaicones.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Knoxbezaicones.jpg/170px-Knoxbezaicones.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Knoxbezaicones.jpg/255px-Knoxbezaicones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Knoxbezaicones.jpg/340px-Knoxbezaicones.jpg 2x" data-file-width="844" data-file-height="1056" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Knoxbezaicones.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1559, <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> returned from ministering in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> to lead the <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> reformation in Scotland.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 16th century, Scotland underwent a <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a> that created a predominantly Calvinist national Kirk, which became Presbyterian in outlook and severely reduced the powers of bishops. In the earlier part of the century, the teachings of first <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a> and then <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> began to influence Scotland, particularly through Scottish scholars, often training for the priesthood, who had visited Continental universities. The Lutheran preacher <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Hamilton_(martyr)" title="Patrick Hamilton (martyr)">Patrick Hamilton</a> was executed for heresy in <a href="/wiki/St._Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Andrews">St. Andrews</a> in 1528.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The execution of others, especially the <a href="/wiki/Zwingli" class="mw-redirect" title="Zwingli">Zwingli</a>-influenced <a href="/wiki/George_Wishart" title="George Wishart">George Wishart</a>, who was burnt at the stake on the orders of Cardinal Beaton in 1546, angered Protestants. Wishart\'s supporters assassinated Beaton soon after and seized St. Andrews Castle, which they held for a year before they were defeated with the help of French forces. The survivors, including chaplain <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>, were condemned to be galley slaves in France, stoking resentment of the French and creating martyrs for the Protestant cause.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Limited toleration and the influence of exiled Scots and Protestants in other countries, led to the expansion of Protestantism, with a group of lairds declaring themselves <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_the_Congregation" title="Lords of the Congregation">Lords of the Congregation</a> in 1557 and representing their interests politically. The collapse of the French alliance and English intervention in 1560 meant that a relatively small, but highly influential, group of Protestants were in a position to impose reform on the Scottish church. A confession of faith, rejecting papal jurisdiction and the mass, was adopted by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation_Parliament" title="Scottish Reformation Parliament">Parliament in 1560</a>, while the young Mary, Queen of Scots, was still in France.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Knox, having escaped the galleys and spent time in Geneva as a follower of Calvin, emerged as the most significant figure of the period. The Calvinism of the reformers led by Knox resulted in a settlement that adopted a Presbyterian system and rejected most of the elaborate trappings of the medieval church. The reformed Kirk gave considerable power to local lairds, who often had control over the appointment of the clergy. There were widespread, but generally orderly outbreaks of <a href="/wiki/Iconoclasm" title="Iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a>. At this point the majority of the population was probably still Catholic in persuasion and the Kirk found it difficult to penetrate the Highlands and Islands, but began a gradual process of conversion and consolidation that, compared with reformations elsewhere, was conducted with relatively little persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Women shared in the religiosity of the day. The egalitarian and emotional aspects of Calvinism appealed to men and women alike. Historian Alasdair Raffe finds that, "Men and women were thought equally likely to be among the elect....Godly men valued the prayers and conversation of their female co-religionists, and this reciprocity made for loving marriages and close friendships between men and women." Furthermore, there was an increasingly intense relationship In the pious bonds between minister and his women parishioners. For the first time, laywomen gained numerous new religious roles,And took a prominent place in prayer societies.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_century">17th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 17th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a></div>\n<p>In 1603, <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI King of Scots</a> inherited the throne of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">Kingdom of England</a>, and became King James I of England, leaving <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> for London, uniting England and Scotland under one monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The Union was a <a href="/wiki/Personal_union" title="Personal union">personal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dynastic_union" title="Dynastic union">dynastic union</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">Crowns</a> remaining both distinct and separate\xe2\x80\x94despite James\'s best efforts to create a new "imperial" throne of "<a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The acquisition of the Irish crown along with the English, facilitated a process of settlement by Scots in what was historically the most troublesome area of the kingdom in <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, with perhaps 50,000 Scots settling in the province by the mid-17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Attempts to found a Scottish colony in North America in <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a> were largely unsuccessful, with insufficient funds and willing colonists.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms_and_the_Puritan_Commonwealth">Wars of the Three Kingdoms and the Puritan Commonwealth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Wars of the Three Kingdoms and the Puritan Commonwealth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a></div>\n<h4><span id="Bishops.27_Wars"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bishops\'_Wars">Bishops\' Wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bishops&#039; Wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bishops%27_Wars" title="Bishops&#39; Wars">Bishops\' Wars</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg/220px-Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg/330px-Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg/440px-Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="520" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Riot_against_Anglican_prayer_book_1637.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/St_Giles_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="St Giles Cathedral">St. Giles</a> riot initiated by <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Geddes" title="Jenny Geddes">Jenny Geddes</a> sparked off the Bishops\' Wars.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although James had tried to get the Scottish Church to accept some of the High Church Anglicanism of his southern kingdom, he met with limited success. His son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, took matters further, introducing an English-style Prayer Book into the Scottish church in 1637. This resulted in anger and widespread rioting. (The story goes that it was initiated by a certain <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Geddes" title="Jenny Geddes">Jenny Geddes</a> who threw a stool in <a href="/wiki/St_Giles_Cathedral" class="mw-redirect" title="St Giles Cathedral">St Giles Cathedral</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991p203_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991p203-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Representatives of various sections of Scottish society drew up the <a href="/wiki/National_Covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="National Covenant">National Covenant</a> in 1638, objecting to the King\'s liturgical innovations. In November of the same year matters were taken even further, when at a meeting of the General Assembly in Glasgow the Scottish bishops were formally expelled from the Church, which was then established on a full Presbyterian basis.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991p205-6_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991p205-6-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Charles gathered a military force; but as neither side wished to push the matter to a full military conflict, a temporary settlement was concluded at <a href="/wiki/Berwick-upon-Tweed#English_rule" title="Berwick-upon-Tweed">Pacification of Berwick</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991p205-6_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991p205-6-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Matters remained unresolved until 1640 when, in a renewal of hostilities, Charles\'s northern forces were defeated by the Scots at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Newburn" title="Battle of Newburn">Battle of Newburn</a> to the west of Newcastle.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp208-9_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp208-9-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> During the course of these Bishops\' Wars Charles tried to raise an army of Irish Catholics, but was forced to back down after a storm of protest in Scotland and England. The backlash from this venture provoked a <a href="/wiki/Irish_Rebellion_of_1641" title="Irish Rebellion of 1641">rebellion in Ireland</a> and Charles was forced to appeal to the English Parliament for funds. Parliament\'s demands for reform in England eventually resulted in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. This series of civil wars that engulfed England, Ireland and Scotland in the 1640s and 1650s is known to modern historians as the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp209-10_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp209-10-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Covenanters" class="mw-redirect" title="Covenanters">Covenanters</a> meanwhile, were left governing Scotland, where they raised a large army of their own and tried to impose their religious settlement on <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Episcopalians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a> in the north of the country. In England his religious policies caused similar resentment and he ruled without recourse to parliament from 1629.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war">Civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg/170px-1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg/255px-1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg/340px-1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1st_Marquess_of_Montrose.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Graham,_1st_Marquess_of_Montrose" title="James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose">James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose</a>, who led a successful pro-royalist campaign in the Highlands in 1644\xe2\x80\x9346.</div></div></div>\n<p>As the civil wars developed, the English <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarians</a> appealed to the Scots Covenanters for military aid against the King. A <a href="/wiki/Solemn_League_and_Covenant" title="Solemn League and Covenant">Solemn League and Covenant</a> was entered into, guaranteeing the Scottish Church settlement and promising further reform in England.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp211-2_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp211-2-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Scottish troops played a major part in the defeat of Charles I, notably at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Marston_Moor" title="Battle of Marston Moor">battle of Marston Moor</a>. An army under the Earl of Leven occupied the North of England for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp213-4_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp213-4-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, not all Scots supported the Covenanter\'s taking arms against their King. In 1644, <a href="/wiki/James_Graham,_1st_Marquess_of_Montrose" title="James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose">James Graham, 1st Marquess of Montrose</a> attempted to raise the Highlands for the King. Few Scots would follow him, but, aided by 1,000 Irish, Highland and Islesmen troops sent by the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Ireland" title="Confederate Ireland">Irish Confederates</a> under <a href="/wiki/Alasdair_Mac_Colla" title="Alasdair Mac Colla">Alasdair MacDonald (MacColla)</a>, and an instinctive genius for mobile warfare, he was stunningly successful. A <a href="/wiki/Scotland_in_the_Wars_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Scotland in the Wars of the Three Kingdoms">Scottish Civil War</a> began in September 1644 with his victory at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tippermuir" title="Battle of Tippermuir">battle of Tippermuir</a>. After a series of victories over poorly trained Covenanter militias, the lowlands were at his mercy. However, at this high point, his army was reduced in size, as MacColla and the Highlanders preferred to continue the war in the north against the Campbells. Shortly after, what was left of his force was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Philiphaugh" title="Battle of Philiphaugh">Battle of Philiphaugh</a>. Escaping to the north, Montrose attempted to continue the struggle with fresh troops; but in July 1646 his army was disbanded after the King surrendered to the Scots army at Newark, and the civil war came to an end.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following year Charles, while he was being held captive in Carisbrooke Castle, entered into an agreement with moderate Scots Presbyterians. In this secret \'<a href="/wiki/Engagers" title="Engagers">Engagement</a>\', the Scots promised military aid in return for the King\'s agreement to implement Presbyterianism in England on a three-year trial basis. The <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Hamilton" title="Duke of Hamilton">Duke of Hamilton</a> led an invasion of England to free the King, but he was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a> in August 1648 at the Battle of Preston.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cromwellian_occupation_and_Restoration">Cromwellian occupation and Restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cromwellian occupation and Restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scotland_under_the_Commonwealth" title="Scotland under the Commonwealth">Scotland under the Commonwealth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Scotland)" title="Restoration (Scotland)">Restoration (Scotland)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/220px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/330px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg/440px-Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cromwell_at_Dunbar_Andrew_Carrick_Gow.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Cromwell at Dunbar" by Andrew Carrick Gow. The battle of Dunbar was a crushing defeat for the Scottish Covenanters</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Charles_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Execution of Charles I">execution of Charles I</a> in 1649 was carried out in the face of objections by the Covenanter government and his son was immediately proclaimed as King <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a> in Edinburgh. Oliver Cromwell led an invasion of Scotland in 1650, and defeated the Scottish army at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunbar_(1650)" title="Battle of Dunbar (1650)">Dunbar</a> and then defeated a Scottish invasion of England at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Worcester" title="Battle of Worcester">Worcester</a> on 3 September 1651 (the anniversary of his victory at Dunbar). Cromwell emerged as the leading figure in the English government and Scotland was occupied by an English force under <a href="/wiki/George_Monck" class="mw-redirect" title="George Monck">George Monck</a>. The country was incorporated into the Puritan-governed <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a> and lost its independent church government, parliament and legal system, but gained access to English markets.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Various attempts were made to legitimise the union, calling representatives from the Scottish burghs and shires to negotiations and to various English parliaments, where they were always under-represented and had little opportunity for dissent. However, final ratification was delayed by Cromwell\'s problems with his various parliaments and the union did not become the subject of an act until 1657 (see <a href="/wiki/Tender_of_Union" title="Tender of Union">Tender of Union</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the death of Cromwell and the regime\'s collapse, Charles II was restored in 1660 and Scotland again became an independent kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp241-5_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp241-5-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Scotland regained its system of law, parliament and kirk, but also the <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_the_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Lords of the Articles">Lords of the Articles</a> (by which the crown managed parliament), bishops and a king who did not visit the country. He ruled largely without reference to Parliament, through a series of commissioners. These began with <a href="/wiki/John_Middleton,_1st_Earl_of_Middleton" title="John Middleton, 1st Earl of Middleton">John, Earl of Middleton</a> and ended with the king\'s brother and heir, <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James, Duke of York</a> (known in Scotland as the Duke of Albany).<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetalp239_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetalp239-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Navigation_Acts" class="mw-redirect" title="English Navigation Acts">English Navigation Acts</a> prevented the Scots engaging in what would have been lucrative trading with England\'s colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The restoration of episcopacy was a source of trouble, particularly in the south-west of the country, an area with strong Presbyterian sympathies. Abandoning the official church, many of the inhabitants began to attend illegal field assemblies, known as <a href="/wiki/Conventicle" title="Conventicle">conventicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Official attempts to suppress these led to a rising in 1679, defeated by <a href="/wiki/James,_Duke_of_Monmouth" class="mw-redirect" title="James, Duke of Monmouth">James, Duke of Monmouth</a>, the King\'s illegitimate son, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bothwell_Bridge" title="Battle of Bothwell Bridge">Battle of Bothwell Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> In the early 1680s a more intense phase of persecution began, later to be called "<a href="/wiki/The_Killing_Time" title="The Killing Time">the Killing Time</a>". When Charles died in 1685 and his brother, a Roman Catholic, succeeded him as <a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James VII of Scotland (and II of England)</a>, matters came to a head.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackie1991p.241_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie1991p.241-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_deposition_of_James_VII">The deposition of James VII</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The deposition of James VII">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution_in_Scotland" title="Glorious Revolution in Scotland">Glorious Revolution in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_1633-1701.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/James_II_1633-1701.jpg/170px-James_II_1633-1701.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/James_II_1633-1701.jpg/255px-James_II_1633-1701.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/James_II_1633-1701.jpg/340px-James_II_1633-1701.jpg 2x" data-file-width="560" data-file-height="678" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_II_1633-1701.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_II_of_England" title="James II of England">James VII of Scotland (and II of England)</a>, who fled the throne in 1688.</div></div></div>\n<p>James put Catholics in key positions in the government and attendance at conventicles was made punishable by death. He disregarded parliament, purged the Council and forced through <a href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious toleration">religious toleration</a> to Roman Catholics, alienating his Protestant subjects. It was believed that the king would be succeeded by his daughter Mary, a Protestant and the wife of <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William of Orange</a>, Stadtholder of the Netherlands, but when in 1688, James produced a male heir, <a href="/wiki/James_Francis_Edward_Stuart" title="James Francis Edward Stuart">James Francis Edward Stuart</a>, it was clear that his policies would outlive him. An invitation by seven leading Englishmen led William to land in England with 40,000 men, and James fled, leading to the almost bloodless "<a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a>". The Estates issued a <i><a href="/wiki/Claim_of_Right_Act_1689" title="Claim of Right Act 1689">Claim of Right</a></i> that suggested that James had forfeited the crown by his actions (in contrast to England, which relied on the legal fiction of an abdication) and offered it to William and Mary, which William accepted, along with limitations on royal power.<sup id="cite_ref-Mackieetal1991pp241-5_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackieetal1991pp241-5-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The final settlement restored Presbyterianism and abolished the bishops who had generally supported James. However, William, who was more tolerant than the Kirk tended to be, passed acts restoring the Episcopalian clergy excluded after the Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nAlthough William\'s supporters dominated the government, there remained a significant following for James, particularly in the Highlands. His cause, which became known as <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobitism</a>, from the Latin <i>(Jacobus)</i> for James, led to a series of risings. An initial Jacobite military attempt was led by <a href="/wiki/John_Graham,_Viscount_Dundee" class="mw-redirect" title="John Graham, Viscount Dundee">John Graham, Viscount Dundee</a>. His forces, almost all Highlanders, defeated William\'s forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Killiecrankie" title="Battle of Killiecrankie">Battle of Killiecrankie</a> in 1689, but they took heavy losses and Dundee was slain in the fighting. Without his leadership the Jacobite army was soon defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dunkeld" title="Battle of Dunkeld">Battle of Dunkeld</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In the aftermath of the Jacobite defeat on 13 February 1692, in an incident since known as the <a href="/wiki/Massacre_of_Glencoe" title="Massacre of Glencoe">Massacre of Glencoe</a>, 38 members of the <a href="/wiki/Clan_MacDonald_of_Glencoe" class="mw-redirect" title="Clan MacDonald of Glencoe">Clan MacDonald of Glencoe</a> were killed by members of the Earl of Argyll\'s Regiment of Foot, on the grounds that they had not been prompt in pledging allegiance to the new monarchs.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_crisis_of_the_1690s">Economic crisis of the 1690s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Economic crisis of the 1690s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seven_ill_years" title="Seven ill years">Seven ill years</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg/170px-New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg/255px-New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg/340px-New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg 2x" data-file-width="972" data-file-height="1191" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Caledonia_in_Darien.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The colony of <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a> on the Isthmus of Darien</div></div></div>\n<p>The closing decade of the 17th century saw the generally favourable economic conditions that had dominated since the Restoration come to an end. There was a slump in trade with the Baltic and France from 1689 to 1691, caused by French protectionism and changes in the Scottish cattle trade, followed by four years of failed harvests (1695, 1696 and 1698-9), an era known as the "seven ill years".<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchison2002pp291&amp;301-2_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchison2002pp291&amp;301-2-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The result was severe famine and depopulation, particularly in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The Parliament of Scotland of 1695 enacted proposals to help the desperate economic situation, including setting up the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Scotland" title="Bank of Scotland">Bank of Scotland</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Company_of_Scotland" title="Company of Scotland">"Company of Scotland Trading to Africa and the Indies"</a> received a charter to raise capital through public subscription.<sup id="cite_ref-Mitchison2002p314_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchison2002p314-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failure_of_Darien_scheme">Failure of Darien scheme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Failure of Darien scheme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Darien_scheme" title="Darien scheme">Darien scheme</a></div>\n<p>With the dream of building a lucrative overseas colony for Scotland, the Company of Scotland invested in the <a href="/wiki/Darien_scheme" title="Darien scheme">Darien scheme</a>, an ambitious plan devised by <a href="/wiki/William_Paterson_(banker)" title="William Paterson (banker)">William Paterson</a> to establish a colony on the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama" title="Isthmus of Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a> in the hope of establishing trade with the Far East.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2004p79_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2004p79-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The Dari\xc3\xa9n scheme won widespread support in Scotland as the landed gentry and the merchant class were in agreement in seeing overseas trade and colonialism as routes to upgrade Scotland\'s economy. Since the capital resources of the Edinburgh merchants and landholder elite were insufficient, the company appealed to middling social ranks, who responded with patriotic fervour to the call for money; the lower classes volunteered as colonists.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> But the English government opposed the idea: involved in the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Grand_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="War of the Grand Alliance">War of the Grand Alliance</a> from 1689 to 1697 against <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, it did not want to offend Spain, which claimed the territory as part of <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Granada" title="New Kingdom of Granada">New Granada</a>. The English investors withdrew. Returning to Edinburgh, the Company raised 400,000 pounds in a few weeks. Three small fleets with a total of 3,000 men eventually set out for Panama in 1698. The exercise proved a disaster. Poorly equipped; beset by incessant rain; under attack by the Spanish from nearby <a href="/wiki/Cartagena,_Colombia" title="Cartagena, Colombia">Cartagena</a>; and refused aid by the English in the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>, the colonists abandoned their project in 1700. Only 1,000 survived and only one ship managed to return to Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Richards2004p79_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2004p79-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="18th_century">18th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 18th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Great_Britain_(1707%E2%80%931800).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Great_Britain_%281707%E2%80%931800%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Great_Britain_(1707%E2%80%931800).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Union flag, combining the <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="St George&#39;s Cross">Cross of St George</a> of England, with the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Scotland" title="Flag of Scotland">Cross of St. Andrew</a> of Scotland.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scotland was a poor rural, agricultural society with a population of 1.3 million in 1755. Although Scotland lost home rule, the Union allowed it to break free of a stultifying system and opened the way for the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish enlightenment">Scottish enlightenment</a> as well as a great expansion of trade and increase in opportunity and wealth. Edinburgh economist <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> concluded in 1776 that "By the union with England, the middling and inferior ranks of people in Scotland gained a complete deliverance from the power of an aristocracy which had always before oppressed them."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Israel" title="Jonathan Israel">Jonathan Israel</a> holds that the Union "proved a decisive catalyst politically and economically," by allowing ambitious Scots entry on an equal basis to a rich expanding empire and its increasing trade.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland\'s transformation into a rich leader of modern industry came suddenly and unexpectedly in the next 150 years, following its union with England in 1707 and its integration with the advanced English and imperial economies.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_Hamilton_1963_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_Hamilton_1963-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The transformation was led by two cities that grew rapidly after 1770. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Glasgow" title="History of Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, on the river Clyde, was the base for the tobacco and sugar trade with an emerging textile industry. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Edinburgh" title="History of Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a> was the administrative and intellectual centre where the Scottish Enlightenment was chiefly based.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_with_England">Union with England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Union with England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a></div>\n<p>By the start of the 18th century, a <a href="/wiki/Political_union" title="Political union">political union</a> between Scotland and England became politically and economically attractive, promising to open up the much larger markets of England, as well as those of the growing English Empire. With economic stagnation since the late 17th century, which was particularly acute in 1704; the country depended more and more heavily on sales of cattle and linen to England, who used this to create pressure for a union.<sup id="cite_ref-Smout1964_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smout1964-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Smout1963_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smout1963-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish parliament voted on 6 January 1707, by 110 to 69, to adopt the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union" title="Treaty of Union">Treaty of Union</a>. It was also a full economic union; indeed, most of its 25 articles dealt with economic arrangements for the new state known as "Great Britain". It added 45 Scots to the 513 members of the House of Commons and 16 Scots to the 190 members of the House of Lords, and ended the Scottish parliament. It also replaced the Scottish systems of currency, taxation and laws regulating trade with laws made in London. Scottish law remained separate from English law, and the religious system was not changed. England had about five times the population of Scotland at the time, and about 36 times as much wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-Smout1964_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smout1964-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Campbell1964pp469-77_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell1964pp469-77-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jacobitism">Jacobitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Jacobitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jacobitism" title="Jacobitism">Jacobitism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart,_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart%2C_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3279" data-file-height="4001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Mosman_-_Prince_Charles_Edward_Stuart,_1720_-_1788._Eldest_son_of_Prince_James_Francis_Edward_Stuart_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Charles Edward Stuart</a>, known as <i>The Young Pretender</i> and <i>Bonnie Prince Charlie</i>, who led the \'45 rising</div></div></div>\n<p>Jacobitism was revived by the unpopularity of the union.<sup id="cite_ref-Pittock1998p32_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pittock1998p32-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> In 1708, James Francis Edward Stuart, the son of James VII, who became known as "The Old Pretender", attempted an invasion with a French fleet carrying 6,000 men, but the Royal Navy prevented it from landing troops.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> A more serious attempt occurred in 1715, soon after the death of Anne and the accession of the first Hanoverian king, the eldest son of Sophie, as <a href="/wiki/George_I_of_Great_Britain" title="George I of Great Britain">George I of Great Britain</a>. This rising (known as <i>The \'Fifteen</i>) envisaged simultaneous uprisings in Wales, Devon, and Scotland. However, government arrests forestalled the southern ventures. In Scotland, John Erskine, <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Mar" title="Earl of Mar">Earl of Mar</a>, nicknamed <i>Bobbin\' John</i>, raised the Jacobite clans but proved to be an indecisive leader and an incompetent soldier. Mar captured Perth, but let a smaller government force under the <a href="/wiki/John_Campbell,_2nd_Duke_of_Argyll" title="John Campbell, 2nd Duke of Argyll">Duke of Argyll</a> hold the Stirling plain. Part of Mar\'s army joined up with risings in northern England and southern Scotland, and the Jacobites fought their way into England before being defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Preston_(1715)" title="Battle of Preston (1715)">Battle of Preston</a>, surrendering on 14 November 1715. The day before, Mar had failed to defeat Argyll at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sheriffmuir" title="Battle of Sheriffmuir">Battle of Sheriffmuir</a>. At this point, James belatedly landed in Scotland, but was advised that the cause was hopeless. He fled back to France. An attempted Jacobite invasion with Spanish assistance in 1719 met with little support from the clans and ended in defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Glen_Shiel" title="Battle of Glen Shiel">Battle of Glen Shiel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1745, the Jacobite rising known as <i>The \'Forty-Five</i> began. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Edward_Stuart" title="Charles Edward Stuart">Charles Edward Stuart</a>, son of the <i>Old Pretender</i>, often referred to as <i>Bonnie Prince Charlie</i> or the <i>Young Pretender</i>, landed on the island of <a href="/wiki/Eriskay" title="Eriskay">Eriskay</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Several clans unenthusiastically joined him. At the outset he was successful, taking Edinburgh<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> and then defeating the only government army in Scotland at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Prestonpans" title="Battle of Prestonpans">Battle of Prestonpans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The Jacobite army marched into England, took Carlisle and advanced as far as south as Derby. However, it became increasingly evident that England would not support a Roman Catholic Stuart monarch. The Jacobite leadership had a crisis of confidence and they retreated to Scotland as two English armies closed in and Hanoverian troops began to return from the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Charles\' position in Scotland began to deteriorate as the Whig supporters rallied and regained control of Edinburgh. After an unsuccessful attempt on Stirling, he retreated north towards Inverness. He was pursued by the <a href="/wiki/Prince_William_Augustus,_Duke_of_Cumberland" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince William Augustus, Duke of Cumberland">Duke of Cumberland</a> and gave battle with an exhausted army at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Culloden" title="Battle of Culloden">Culloden</a> on 16 April 1746, where the Jacobite cause was crushed.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Charles hid in Scotland with the aid of Highlanders until September 1746, when he escaped back to France.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> There were bloody reprisals against his supporters and foreign powers abandoned the Jacobite cause, with the court in exile forced to leave France. The Old Pretender died in 1760 and the Young Pretender, without legitimate issue, in 1788. When his brother, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Benedict_Stuart" title="Henry Benedict Stuart">Henry, Cardinal of York</a>, died in 1807, the Jacobite cause was at an end.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Jacobite_politics">Post-Jacobite politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Post-Jacobite politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg/170px-3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg/255px-3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg/340px-3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="677" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3rdDukeOfArgyll.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Archibald_Campbell,_3rd_Duke_of_Argyll" title="Archibald Campbell, 3rd Duke of Argyll">Archibald Campbell, 3rd Duke of Argyll</a>, and dominant political figure in Scotland, 1720s\xe2\x80\x9361.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Union</a> and the demise of Jacobitism, access to London and the Empire opened up very attractive career opportunities for ambitious middle-class and upper-class Scots, who seized the chance to become entrepreneurs, intellectuals, and soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Thousands of Scots, mainly Lowlanders, took up positions of power in politics, civil service, the army and navy, trade, economics, colonial enterprises and other areas across the nascent <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>. Historian Neil Davidson notes that \xe2\x80\x9cafter 1746 there was an entirely new level of participation by Scots in political life, particularly outside Scotland\xe2\x80\x9d. Davidson also states that \xe2\x80\x9cfar from being \xe2\x80\x98peripheral\xe2\x80\x99 to the British economy, Scotland \xe2\x80\x93 or more precisely, the Lowlands \xe2\x80\x93 lay at its core\xe2\x80\x9d.<sup id="cite_ref-Davidson2000pp94-5_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson2000pp94-5-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> British officials especially appreciated Scottish soldiers. As the Secretary of War told Parliament in 1751, "I am for having always in our army as many Scottish soldiers as possible...because they are generally more hardy and less mutinous".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The national policy of aggressively recruiting Scots for senior civilian positions stirred up resentment among Englishmen, ranging from violent diatribes by <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkes" title="John Wilkes">John Wilkes</a>, to vulgar jokes and obscene cartoons in the popular press,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> and the haughty ridicule by intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> that was much resented by Scots. In his great <i>Dictionary</i> Johnson defined oats as, "a grain, which in England is generally given to horses, but in Scotland supports the people." To which Lord Elibank retorted, "Very true, and where will you find such men and such horses?"<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scottish politics in the late 18th century was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" class="mw-redirect" title="Whig (British political party)">Whigs</a>, with the benign management of <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Campbell,_3rd_Duke_of_Argyll" title="Archibald Campbell, 3rd Duke of Argyll">Archibald Campbell, 3rd Duke of Argyll</a> (1682\xe2\x80\x931761), who was in effect the "viceroy of Scotland" from the 1720s until his death in 1761. Scotland generally supported the king with enthusiasm during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dundas,_1st_Viscount_Melville" title="Henry Dundas, 1st Viscount Melville">Henry Dundas</a> (1742\xe2\x80\x931811) dominated political affairs in the latter part of the century. Dundas put a brake on intellectual and social change through his ruthless manipulation of patronage in alliance with Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">William Pitt the Younger</a>, until he lost power in 1806.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The main unit of local government was the parish, and since it was also part of the church, the elders imposed public humiliation for what the locals considered immoral behaviour, including fornication, drunkenness, wife beating, cursing and Sabbath breaking. The main focus was on the poor and the landlords ("lairds") and gentry, and their servants, were not subject to the parish\'s control. The policing system weakened after 1800 and disappeared in most places by the 1850s.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine2001pp84-89_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine2001pp84-89-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_of_the_clan_system">Collapse of the clan system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Collapse of the clan system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clan" title="Scottish clan">Scottish clan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg/220px-Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg/330px-Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg/440px-Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crofts_at_Borreraig_-_geograph.org.uk_-_137969.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Croft_(land)" title="Croft (land)">Crofts</a> at <a href="/wiki/Borreraig" title="Borreraig">Borreraig</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Skye" title="Skye">Skye</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the battle of Culloden the leaders were declared to be traitors, with Jacobite officers executed and many of the rebel soldiers shipped to the colonies as indentured servants. Key laws included the <a href="/wiki/Dress_Act_1746" title="Dress Act 1746">Dress Act 1746</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Proscription_1746" title="Act of Proscription 1746">Act of Proscription 1746</a>, and especially the <a href="/wiki/Heritable_Jurisdictions_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Heritable Jurisdictions Act">Heritable Jurisdictions Act</a> of 1746. Parliament also banned the bearing of arms and the wearing of tartans, and limited the activities of the Episcopalian Church. After a generation the Highlands had been transformed and the laws were no longer needed; they were mostly repealed.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historians debate whether the dramatic changes merely reflect long-term trends that were more-or-less inevitable, or whether government intervention played the decisive role in changing the goals and roles of the chiefs.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> As Conway (2006) concludes, the new policies "went far beyond earlier efforts to promote economic development in the Highlands and ... represented the first real endeavour to transform the region\'s social system ... the post-rebellion legislation certainly seems to have accelerated the change."<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> However Devine (1999) and Ray (2001) argue that long-term economic and social changes were already undermining the clan system.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The major result of these changes were the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Clearances" title="Highland Clearances">Highland Clearances</a>, in which many of the population of the Highlands were evicted as lands were <a href="/wiki/Enclosure" title="Enclosure">enclosed</a>, principally so that they could be used for sheep farming. The clearances followed patterns of agricultural change throughout Britain, but were notorious as a result of the late timing, the lack of legal protection for year-by-year tenants under <a href="/wiki/Scots_law" title="Scots law">Scots law</a>, the abruptness of the change from the traditional clan system, and the brutality of some evictions.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>  Many of those who remained were now <a href="/wiki/Croft_(land)" title="Croft (land)">crofters</a>: poor families living on "crofts"\xe2\x80\x94very small rented farms with indefinite tenure used to raise various crops and animals, with <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelping</a> industry (where men burned kelp for the ashes), fishing, spinning of linen and military service as important sources of revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The era of the Napoleonic wars, 1790\xe2\x80\x931815, brought prosperity, optimism, and economic growth to the Highlands. The economy grew thanks to higher wages, as well as large-scale infrastructure spending such as the <a href="/wiki/Caledonian_Canal" title="Caledonian Canal">Caledonian Canal</a> project. On the East Coast, farmlands were improved, and high prices for cattle brought money to the community. Service in the Army was also attractive to young men from Highlands, who sent pay home and retired there with their army pensions, but the prosperity ended after 1815, and long-run negative factors began to undermine the economic position of the crofters.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enlightenment">Enlightenment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Enlightenment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AdamSmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/170px-AdamSmith.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/255px-AdamSmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/AdamSmith.jpg/340px-AdamSmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1456" data-file-height="2173" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AdamSmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, the father of modern economics.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a></div>\n<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Israel" title="Jonathan Israel">Jonathan Israel</a> argues that by 1750 Scotland\'s major cities had created an intellectual infrastructure of mutually supporting institutions, such as universities, reading societies, libraries, periodicals, museums and masonic lodges. The Scottish network was "predominantly liberal Calvinist, Newtonian, and \'design\' oriented in character which played a major role in the further development of the transatlantic Enlightenment ."<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HermanTwo_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HermanTwo-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In France <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> said "we look to Scotland for all our ideas of civilization," and the Scots in turn paid close attention to French ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Historian Bruce Lenman says their "central achievement was a new capacity to recognize and interpret social patterns."<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The first major philosopher of the Scottish Enlightenment was <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Francis Hutcheson</a>, who held the Chair of Philosophy at the University of Glasgow from 1729 to 1746. A moral philosopher who produced alternatives to the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, one of his major contributions to world thought was the <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consequentialism" title="Consequentialism">consequentialist</a> principle that virtue is that which provides, in his words, "the greatest happiness for the greatest numbers". Much of what is incorporated in the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> (the nature of knowledge, evidence, experience, and causation) and some modern attitudes towards the relationship between science and religion were developed by his prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9s <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Denby_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denby-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Hume became a major figure in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">skeptical philosophical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empiricist" class="mw-redirect" title="Empiricist">empiricist</a> traditions of philosophy. He and other Scottish Enlightenment thinkers developed what he called a \'<a href="/wiki/Science_of_man" title="Science of man">science of man</a>\',<sup id="cite_ref-Magnusson_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> which was expressed historically in works by authors including <a href="/wiki/James_Burnett" class="mw-redirect" title="James Burnett">James Burnett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ferguson" title="Adam Ferguson">Adam Ferguson</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Millar_(philosopher)" title="John Millar (philosopher)">John Millar</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">William Robertson</a>, all of whom merged a scientific study of how humans behave in ancient and primitive cultures with a strong awareness of the determining forces of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>. Modern sociology largely originated from this movement<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> and Hume\'s philosophical concepts that directly influenced <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> (and thus the US Constitution) and when popularised by <a href="/wiki/Dugald_Stewart" title="Dugald Stewart">Dugald Stewart</a>, would be the basis of classical <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Adam Smith published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wealth_of_Nations" title="The Wealth of Nations">The Wealth of Nations</a></i>, often considered the first work on modern economics. It had an immediate impact on British economic policy and in the 21st century still framed discussions on <a href="/wiki/Globalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Globalisation">globalisation</a> and tariffs.<sup id="cite_ref-Fry_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fry-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> The focus of the Scottish Enlightenment ranged from intellectual and economic matters to the specifically scientific as in the work of the physician and chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Cullen" title="William Cullen">William Cullen</a>, the agriculturalist and economist <a href="/wiki/James_Anderson_of_Hermiston" title="James Anderson of Hermiston">James Anderson</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> chemist and physician <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Black" title="Joseph Black">Joseph Black</a>, natural historian <a href="/wiki/John_Walker_(natural_historian)" title="John Walker (natural historian)">John Walker</a><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/James_Hutton" title="James Hutton">James Hutton</a>, the first modern geologist.<sup id="cite_ref-Denby_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denby-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Repcheck_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Repcheck-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginnings_of_industrialisation">Beginnings of industrialisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Beginnings of industrialisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Scotland" title="Economic history of Scotland">Economic history of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank,_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg/170px-Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg/255px-Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg/340px-Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank%2C_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Former_Head_Office_of_the_British_Linen_Bank,_St._Andrew_Square_Edinburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Former Head Office of the <a href="/wiki/British_Linen_Bank" title="British Linen Bank">British Linen Bank</a> in St Andrew Square, Edinburgh. Now offices of the Bank of Scotland.</div></div></div>\n<p>With tariffs with England now abolished, the potential for trade for Scottish merchants was considerable. However, Scotland in 1750 was still a poor rural, agricultural society with a population of 1.3 million.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Some progress was visible: agriculture in the Lowlands was steadily upgraded after 1700 and standards remained high.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> there were the sales of linen and cattle to England, the cash flows from military service, and the tobacco trade that was dominated by Glasgow <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_Lords" title="Tobacco Lords">Tobacco Lords</a> after 1740.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Merchants who profited from the American trade began investing in leather, textiles, iron, coal, sugar, rope, sailcloth, glassworks, breweries, and soapworks, setting the foundations for the city\'s emergence as a leading industrial centre after 1815.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The tobacco trade collapsed during the American Revolution (1776\xe2\x80\x9383), when its sources were cut off by the British blockade of American ports. However, trade with the West Indies began to make up for the loss of the tobacco business,<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell1964pp469-77_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell1964pp469-77-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> reflecting the British demand for sugar and the demand in the West Indies for herring and linen goods.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Linen was Scotland\'s premier industry in the 18th century and formed the basis for the later cotton, jute,<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> and woollen industries.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Scottish industrial policy was made by the Board of Trustees for Fisheries and Manufactures in Scotland, which sought to build an economy complementary, not competitive, with England. Since England had woollens, this meant linen. Encouraged and subsidised by the Board of Trustees so it could compete with German products, merchant entrepreneurs became dominant in all stages of linen manufacturing and built up the market share of Scottish linens, especially in the American colonial market.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> The British Linen Company, established in 1746, was the largest firm in the Scottish linen industry in the 18th century, exporting linen to England and America. As a joint-stock company, it had the right to raise funds through the issue of promissory notes or bonds. With its bonds functioning as bank notes, the company gradually moved into the business of lending and discounting to other linen manufacturers, and in the early 1770s banking became its main activity.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> It joined the established Scottish banks such as the Bank of Scotland (Edinburgh, 1695) and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Bank_of_Scotland" title="Royal Bank of Scotland">Royal Bank of Scotland</a> (Edinburgh, 1727).<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Glasgow would soon follow and Scotland had a flourishing financial system by the end of the century. There were over 400 branches, amounting to one office per 7,000 people, double the level in England, where banks were also more heavily regulated. Historians have emphasised that the flexibility and dynamism of the Scottish banking system contributed significantly to the rapid development of the economy in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>German sociologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a> mentioned Scottish Presbyterianism in <a href="/wiki/The_Protestant_Ethic_and_the_Spirit_of_Capitalism" title="The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism">The Protestant Ethic and the Spirit of Capitalism</a> (1905), and many scholars in recent decades argued that "this worldly asceticism" of Calvinism was integral to Scotland\'s rapid economic modernisation.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_fragmentation">Religious fragmentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Religious fragmentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_Scotland" title="History of Christianity in Scotland">History of Christianity in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EbenezerErskine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/EbenezerErskine.jpg/170px-EbenezerErskine.jpg" width="170" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/EbenezerErskine.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="221" data-file-height="280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EbenezerErskine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Erskine" title="Ebenezer Erskine">Ebenezer Erskine</a> whose actions led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/First_Secession" title="First Secession">Secession Church</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 1690s the Presbyterian establishment purged the land of Episcopalians and heretics, and made blasphemy a capital crime. Thomas Aitkenhead, the son of an Edinburgh surgeon, aged 18, was indicted for blasphemy by order of the Privy Council for calling the New Testament "The History of the Imposter Christ"; he was hanged in 1696.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Their extremism led to a reaction known as the "Moderate" cause that ultimately prevailed and opened the way for liberal thinking in the cities.\n</p><p>The early 18th century saw the beginnings of a fragmentation of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>. These fractures were prompted by issues of government and patronage, but reflected a wider division between the hard-line <a href="/wiki/Evangelicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelicals">Evangelicals</a> and the theologically more tolerant <a href="/wiki/Moderate_Party_(Scotland)" title="Moderate Party (Scotland)">Moderate Party</a>. The battle was over fears of fanaticism by the former and the promotion of Enlightenment ideas by the latter. The Patronage Act of 1712 was a major blow to the evangelicals, for it meant that local landlords could choose the minister, not the members of the congregation.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Schisms erupted as the evangelicals left the main body, starting in 1733 with the <a href="/wiki/First_Secession" title="First Secession">First Secession</a> headed by figures including <a href="/wiki/Ebenezer_Erskine" title="Ebenezer Erskine">Ebenezer Erskine</a>. The second schism in 1761 lead to the foundation of the independent <a href="/wiki/Relief_Church" title="Relief Church">Relief Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> These churches gained strength in the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Revival" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Revival">Evangelical Revival</a> of the later 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Ditchfield1998p91_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ditchfield1998p91-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> A key result was the main Presbyterian church was in the hands of the Moderate faction, which provided critical support for the Enlightenment in the cities.\n</p><p>\nLong after the triumph of the Church of Scotland in the Lowlands, Highlanders and Islanders clung to an old-fashioned Christianity infused with animistic folk beliefs and practices.
