The remoteness of the region and the lack of a Gaelic-speaking clergy undermined the missionary efforts of the established church. The later 18th century saw some success, owing to the efforts of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_SPCK" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish SPCK">SSPCK</a> missionaries and to the disruption of traditional society.<sup id="cite_ref-Robb1990_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robb1990-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Catholicism had been reduced to the fringes of the country, particularly the Gaelic-speaking areas of the Highlands and Islands. Conditions also grew worse for Catholics after the Jacobite rebellions and Catholicism was reduced to little more than a poorly run mission. Also important was Episcopalianism, which had retained supporters through the civil wars and changes of regime in the 17th century. Since most Episcopalians had given their support to the Jacobite rebellions in the early 18th century, they also suffered a decline in fortunes.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature of Scotland">Literature of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_burns.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Robert_burns.jpg/220px-Robert_burns.jpg" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Robert_burns.jpg/330px-Robert_burns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Robert_burns.jpg/440px-Robert_burns.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="639" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_burns.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> (1759\xe2\x80\x9396) exalted as Scotland\'s national poet.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Scotland increasingly adopted the English language and wider cultural norms, its literature developed a distinct national identity and began to enjoy an international reputation. <a href="/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(poet)" title="Allan Ramsay (poet)">Allan Ramsay</a> (1686\xe2\x80\x931758) laid the foundations of a reawakening of interest in older Scottish literature, as well as leading the trend for pastoral poetry, helping to develop the <a href="/wiki/Habbie_stanza" class="mw-redirect" title="Habbie stanza">Habbie stanza</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Poetic_form" class="mw-redirect" title="Poetic form">poetic form</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Macpherson" title="James Macpherson">James Macpherson</a> was the first Scottish poet to gain an international reputation, claiming to have found poetry written by <a href="/wiki/Ossian" title="Ossian">Ossian</a>, he published translations that acquired international popularity, being proclaimed as a Celtic equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical</a> <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epics</a>. <i>Fingal</i> written in 1762 was speedily translated into many European languages, and its deep appreciation of natural beauty and the melancholy tenderness of its treatment of the ancient legend did more than any single work to bring about the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic movement</a> in European, and especially in <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, literature, influencing <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_von_Herder" class="mw-redirect" title="Johann Gottfried von Herder">Herder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Eventually it became clear that the poems were not direct translations from the Gaelic, but flowery adaptations made to suit the aesthetic expectations of his audience.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> Both the major literary figures of the following century, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> and Walter Scott, would be highly influenced by the Ossian cycle. Burns, an Ayrshire poet and lyricist, is widely regarded as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_poets" class="mw-redirect" title="List of national poets">national poet</a> of Scotland and a major figure in the Romantic movement. As well as making original compositions, Burns also collected <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk songs</a> from across Scotland, often revising or <a href="/wiki/Literary_adaptation" title="Literary adaptation">adapting</a> them. His poem (and song) "<a href="/wiki/Auld_Lang_Syne" title="Auld Lang Syne">Auld Lang Syne</a>" is often sung at <a href="/wiki/Hogmanay" title="Hogmanay">Hogmanay</a> (the last day of the year), and "<a href="/wiki/Scots_Wha_Hae" title="Scots Wha Hae">Scots Wha Hae</a>" served for a long time as an unofficial <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_Scotland" title="History of education in Scotland">History of education in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_College_quadrangle,_Edinburgh.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/220px-Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/330px-Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG/440px-Old_College_quadrangle%2C_Edinburgh.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Old_College_quadrangle,_Edinburgh.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Old_College,_University_of_Edinburgh" title="Old College, University of Edinburgh">Old College, University of Edinburgh</a>, rebuilt in 1789 according to plans drawn up by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Adam" title="Robert Adam">Robert Adam</a></div></div></div>\n<p>A legacy of the Reformation in Scotland was the aim of having a school in every parish, which was underlined by an act of the Scottish parliament in 1696 (reinforced in 1801). In rural communities this obliged local landowners (heritors) to provide a schoolhouse and pay a schoolmaster, while ministers and local <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">presbyteries</a> oversaw the quality of the education. The headmaster or "dominie" was often university educated and enjoyed high local prestige.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The kirk schools were active in the rural lowlands but played a minor role in the Highlands, the islands, and in the fast-growing industrial towns and cities.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> The schools taught in English, not in Gaelic, because that language was seen as a leftover of Catholicism and was not an expression of Scottish nationalism.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In cities such as Glasgow the Catholics operated their own schools, which directed their youth into clerical and middle class occupations, as well as religious vocations.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A "democratic myth" emerged in the 19th century to the effect that many a "lad of pairts" had been able to rise up through the system to take high office and that literacy was much more widespread in Scotland than in neighbouring states, particularly England.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Historical research has largely undermined the myth. Kirk schools were not free, attendance was not compulsory and they generally imparted only basic literacy such as the ability to read the Bible. Poor children, starting at age 7, were done by age 8 or 9; the majority were finished by age 11 or 12. The result was widespread basic reading ability; since there was an extra fee for writing, half the people never learned to write. Scots were not significantly better educated than the English and other contemporary nations. A few talented poor boys did go to university, but usually they were helped by aristocratic or gentry sponsors. Most of them became poorly paid teachers or ministers, and none became important figures in the Scottish Enlightenment or the Industrial Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine2001pp96-100_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine2001pp96-100-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nBy the 18th century there were five universities in Scotland, at <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Edinburgh University">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Glasgow University">Glasgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/St._Andrews_University" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Andrews University">St. Andrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College,_Aberdeen" title="King&#39;s College, Aberdeen">King\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marischal_College" title="Marischal College">Marischial</a> Colleges in Aberdeen, compared with only two in England. Originally oriented to clerical and legal training, after the religious and political upheavals of the 17th century they recovered with a lecture-based curriculum that was able to embrace economics and science, offering a high quality liberal education to the sons of the nobility and gentry. It helped the universities to become major centres of medical education and to put Scotland at the forefront of Enlightenment thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardie_elect.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hardie_elect.jpg/220px-Hardie_elect.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hardie_elect.jpg/330px-Hardie_elect.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/64/Hardie_elect.jpg/440px-Hardie_elect.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1734" data-file-height="1337" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hardie_elect.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An election advertisement for Scottish Labour leader <a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Scotland\'s transformation into a rich leader of modern industry came suddenly and unexpectedly.<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_Hamilton_1963_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_Hamilton_1963-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The population grew steadily in the 19th century, from 1,608,000 in the census of 1801 to 2,889,000 in 1851 and 4,472,000 in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The economy, long based on agriculture,<sup id="cite_ref-Henry_Hamilton_1963_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henry_Hamilton_1963-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> began to industrialise after 1790. At first the leading industry, based in the west, was the spinning and weaving of cotton. In 1861, the American Civil War suddenly cut off the supplies of raw cotton and the industry never recovered. Thanks to its many entrepreneurs and engineers, and its large stock of easily mined coal, Scotland became a world centre for engineering, shipbuilding, and locomotive construction, with steel replacing iron after 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Party_politics">Party politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Party politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reform_Act_1832" title="Scottish Reform Act 1832">Scottish Reform Act 1832</a> increased the number of Scottish MPs and significantly widened the franchise to include more of the middle classes. From this point until the end of the century, the Whigs and (after 1859) their successors the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a>, managed to gain a majority of the Westminster Parliamentary seats for Scotland, although these were often outnumbered by the much larger number of English and Welsh Conservatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The English-educated Scottish peer <a href="/wiki/George_Hamilton-Gordon,_4th_Earl_of_Aberdeen" title="George Hamilton-Gordon, 4th Earl of Aberdeen">Lord Aberdeen</a> (1784\xe2\x80\x931860) led a coalition government from 1852\xe2\x80\x935, but in general very few Scots held office in the government.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> From the mid-century there were increasing calls for Home Rule for Scotland and when the Conservative <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gascoyne-Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Gascoyne-Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Lord Salisbury</a> became prime minister in 1885 he responded to pressure by reviving the post of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Scotland" title="Secretary of State for Scotland">Secretary of State for Scotland</a>, which had been in abeyance since 1746.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> He appointed the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gordon-Lennox,_6th_Duke_of_Richmond" title="Charles Gordon-Lennox, 6th Duke of Richmond">Duke of Richmond</a>, a wealthy landowner who was both Chancellor of Aberdeen University and Lord Lieutenant of Banff.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of the century Prime Ministers of Scottish descent included the Tory, <a href="/wiki/Peelite" title="Peelite">Peelite</a> and Liberal <a href="/wiki/William_Gladstone" class="mw-redirect" title="William Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>, who held the office four times between 1868 and 1894.<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The first Scottish Liberal to become prime minister was <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">the Earl of Rosebery</a>, from 1894 to 1895, like Aberdeen before him a product of the English education system.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> In the later 19th century the issue of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a> led to a split among the Liberals, with a minority breaking away to form the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Unionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberal Unionists">Liberal Unionists</a> in 1886.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The growing importance of the working classes was marked by <a href="/wiki/Keir_Hardie" title="Keir Hardie">Keir Hardie</a>\'s success in the <a href="/wiki/Mid_Lanarkshire_by-election,_1888" title="Mid Lanarkshire by-election, 1888">Mid Lanarkshire by-election, 1888</a>, leading to the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Labour_Party_(1888)" title="Scottish Labour Party (1888)">Scottish Labour Party</a>, which was absorbed into the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> in 1895, with Hardie as its first leader.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_expansion">Industrial expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Industrial expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewlanarkNL06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/NewlanarkNL06.jpg/220px-NewlanarkNL06.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/NewlanarkNL06.jpg/330px-NewlanarkNL06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/NewlanarkNL06.jpg/440px-NewlanarkNL06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewlanarkNL06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/New_Lanark" title="New Lanark">New Lanark</a> cotton mill on the banks of the River Clyde, founded in 1786.</div></div></div>\n<p>From about 1790 textiles became the most important industry in the west of Scotland, especially the spinning and weaving of cotton, which flourished until in 1861 the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> cut off the supplies of raw cotton.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> The industry never recovered, but by that time Scotland had developed heavy industries based on its coal and iron resources. The invention of the hot blast for smelting iron (1828) revolutionised the Scottish iron industry. As a result, Scotland became a centre for engineering, shipbuilding and the production of locomotives. Toward the end of the 19th century, steel production largely replaced iron production.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Coal mining continued to grow into the 20th century, producing the fuel to heat homes, factories and drive steam engines locomotives and steamships. By 1914, there were 1,000,000 coal miners in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The stereotype emerged early on of Scottish colliers as brutish, non-religious and socially isolated serfs;<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> that was an exaggeration, for their life style resembled the miners everywhere, with a strong emphasis on masculinity, equalitarianism, group solidarity, and support for radical labour movements.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Britain was the world leader in the construction of railways, and their use to expand trade and coal supplies. The first successful locomotive-powered line in Scotland, between <a href="/wiki/Monklands_(district)" title="Monklands (district)">Monkland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kirkintilloch" title="Kirkintilloch">Kirkintilloch</a>, opened in 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Not only was good passenger service established by the late 1840s, but an excellent network of freight lines reduce the cost of shipping coal, and made products manufactured in Scotland competitive throughout Britain. For example, railways opened the London market to Scottish beef and milk. They enabled the <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen_Angus" class="mw-redirect" title="Aberdeen Angus">Aberdeen Angus</a> to become a cattle breed of worldwide reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> By 1900, Scotland had 3500 miles of railway; their main economic contribution was moving supplies in and product out for heavy industry, especially coal-mining.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_(1881).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/220px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/330px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg/440px-John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_%281881%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1189" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Atkinson_Grimshaw_-_Shipping_on_the_Clyde_(1881).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Shipping on the Clyde", by <a href="/wiki/John_Atkinson_Grimshaw" title="John Atkinson Grimshaw">John Atkinson Grimshaw</a>, 1881.</div></div></div>\n<p>Scotland was already one of the most urbanised societies in Europe by 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> The industrial belt ran across the country from southwest to northeast; by 1900 the four industrialised counties of Lanarkshire, Renfrewshire, Dunbartonshire, and Ayrshire contained 44 per cent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Glasgow became one of the largest cities in the world, and known as "the Second City of the Empire" after London.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Shipbuilding on <a href="/wiki/Clydeside" class="mw-redirect" title="Clydeside">Clydeside</a> (the river Clyde through Glasgow and other points) began when the first small yards were opened in 1712 at the <a href="/wiki/Scotts_Shipbuilding_and_Engineering_Company" title="Scotts Shipbuilding and Engineering Company">Scott family\'s</a> shipyard at Greenock. After 1860, the Clydeside shipyards specialised in steamships made of iron (after 1870, made of steel), which rapidly replaced the wooden sailing vessels of both the merchant fleets and the battle fleets of the world. It became the world\'s pre-eminent shipbuilding centre. <i>Clydebuilt</i> became an industry benchmark of quality, and the river\'s shipyards were given contracts for warships.<sup id="cite_ref-Shields1949_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shields1949-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_health_and_welfare">Public health and welfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Public health and welfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The industrial developments, while they brought work and wealth, were so rapid that housing, town-planning, and provision for public health did not keep pace with them, and for a time living conditions in some of the towns and cities were notoriously bad, with overcrowding, high infant mortality, and growing rates of tuberculosis.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> The companies attracted rural workers, as well as immigrants from Catholic Ireland, by inexpensive company housing that was a dramatic move upward from the inner-city slums. This paternalistic policy led many owners to endorse government sponsored housing programs as well as self-help projects among the respectable working class.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intellectual_life">Intellectual life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Intellectual life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/170px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg" width="170" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/255px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg/340px-Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1649" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Henry_Raeburn_-_Portrait_of_Sir_Walter_Scott.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Waverley_Novels" title="Waverley Novels">Waverley Novels</a> helped define Scottish identity in the 19th century.</div></div></div>\n<p>While the Scottish Enlightenment is traditionally considered to have concluded toward the end of the 18th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Magnusson_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> disproportionately large Scottish contributions to British science and letters continued for another 50 years or more, thanks to such figures as the mathematicians and physicists <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a>, and the engineers and inventors <a href="/wiki/James_Watt" title="James Watt">James Watt</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Murdoch" title="William Murdoch">William Murdoch</a>, whose work was critical to the technological developments of the Industrial Revolution throughout Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In literature the most successful figure of the mid-nineteenth century was <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>, who began as a poet and also collected and published Scottish ballads. His first prose work, <a href="/wiki/Waverley_(novel)" title="Waverley (novel)">Waverley</a> in 1814, is often called the first <a href="/wiki/Historical_novel" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical novel">historical novel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> It launched a highly successful career that probably more than any other helped define and popularise Scottish cultural identity.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> In the late 19th century, a number of Scottish-born authors achieved international reputations. <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>\'s work included the <a href="/wiki/Urban_Gothic" title="Urban Gothic">urban Gothic</a> novella <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Case_of_Dr_Jekyll_and_Mr_Hyde" title="Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde">Strange Case of Dr Jekyll and Mr Hyde</a></i> (1886), and played a major part in developing the historical adventure in books like <i><a href="/wiki/Kidnapped_(novel)" title="Kidnapped (novel)">Kidnapped</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle">Arthur Conan Doyle</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">Sherlock Holmes</a></i> stories helped found the tradition of detective fiction. The "<a href="/wiki/Kailyard_school" title="Kailyard school">kailyard tradition</a>" at the end of the century, brought elements of <a href="/wiki/Fantasy" title="Fantasy">fantasy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> back into fashion as can be seen in the work of figures like <a href="/wiki/J._M._Barrie" title="J. M. Barrie">J. M. Barrie</a>, most famous for his creation of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan" title="Peter Pan">Peter Pan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_MacDonald" title="George MacDonald">George MacDonald</a>, whose works, including <i><a href="/wiki/Phantasies" title="Phantasies">Phantasies</a></i>, played a major part in the creation of the fantasy genre.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scotland also played a major part in the development of art and architecture. The <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_School" title="Glasgow School">Glasgow School</a>, which developed in the late 19th century, and flourished in the early 20th century, produced a distinctive blend of influences including the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Revival" title="Celtic Revival">Celtic Revival</a> the <a href="/wiki/Arts_and_Crafts_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Arts and Crafts Movement">Arts and Crafts Movement</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japonisme" class="mw-redirect" title="Japonisme">Japonisme</a>, which found favour throughout the <a href="/wiki/Modern_art" title="Modern art">modern art</a> world of continental <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and helped define the <a href="/wiki/Art_Nouveau" title="Art Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> style. Among the most prominent members were the loose collective of The Four: acclaimed <a href="/wiki/Architect" title="Architect">architect</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Rennie_Mackintosh" title="Charles Rennie Mackintosh">Charles Rennie Mackintosh</a>, his wife the <a href="/wiki/Painting" title="Painting">painter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Studio_glass" title="Studio glass">glass artist</a> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_MacDonald_(artist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Margaret MacDonald (artist)">Margaret MacDonald</a>, her sister the artist <a href="/wiki/Frances_MacDonald" title="Frances MacDonald">Frances</a>, and her husband, the artist and teacher <a href="/wiki/Herbert_MacNair" title="Herbert MacNair">Herbert MacNair</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_romanticism_of_the_Highlands">Decline and romanticism of the Highlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Decline and romanticism of the Highlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Highlands_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Highlands of Scotland">Highlands of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_IV_in_kilt,_by_Wilkie.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg/170px-George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg" width="170" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg/255px-George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg/340px-George_IV_in_kilt%2C_by_Wilkie.jpg 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_IV_in_kilt,_by_Wilkie.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Wilkie_(artist)" title="David Wilkie (artist)">David Wilkie</a>\'s flattering portrait of the <a href="/wiki/Kilt" title="Kilt">kilted</a> <a href="/wiki/King_George_IV" class="mw-redirect" title="King George IV">King George IV</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>This period saw a process of rehabilitation for highland culture. Tartan had already been adopted for highland regiments in the British army, which poor highlanders joined in large numbers until the end of the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a> in 1815, but by the 19th century it had largely been abandoned by the ordinary people. In the 1820s, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic revival</a>, tartan and the kilt were adopted by members of the social elite, not just in Scotland, but across Europe,<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sievers2007_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sievers2007-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> prompted by the popularity of Macpherson\'s Ossian cycle<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> and then Walter Scott\'s Waverley novels. The world paid attention to their literary redefinition of Scottishness, as they forged an image largely based on characteristics in polar opposition to those associated with England and modernity. This new identity made it possible for Scottish culture to become integrated into a wider European and North American context, not to mention tourist sites, but it also locked in a sense of "otherness" which Scotland began to shed only in the late 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> Scott\'s "staging" of the royal <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_King_George_IV_to_Scotland" title="Visit of King George IV to Scotland">Visit of King George IV to Scotland</a> in 1822 and the king\'s wearing of tartan, resulted in a massive upsurge in demand for kilts and tartans that could not be met by the Scottish linen industry. The designation of individual clan tartans was largely defined in this period and became a major symbol of Scottish identity.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The fashion for all things Scottish was maintained by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, who helped secure the identity of Scotland as a tourist resort, with <a href="/wiki/Balmoral_Castle" title="Balmoral Castle">Balmoral Castle</a> in Aberdeenshire becoming a major royal residence from 1852.<sup id="cite_ref-Sievers2007_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sievers2007-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite these changes the highlands remained very poor and traditional, with few connections to the uplift of the Scottish Enlightenment and little role in the Industrial Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> A handful of powerful families, typified by the dukes of <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Argyll" title="Duke of Argyll">Argyll</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Atholl" title="Duke of Atholl">Atholl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Buccleuch" title="Duke of Buccleuch">Buccleuch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Sutherland" title="Duke of Sutherland">Sutherland</a>, owned the best lands and controlled local political, legal and economic affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Particularly after the end of the boom created by the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">Revolutionary</a> and Napoleonic Wars (1790\xe2\x80\x931815), these landlords needed cash to maintain their position in London society, and had less need of soldiers. They turned to money rents, displaced farmers to raise sheep, and downplayed the traditional patriarchal relationship that had historically sustained the clans. This was exacerbated after the repeal of the <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> in mid-century, when Britain adopted a free trade policy, and grain imports from America undermined the profitability of crop production.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish potato famine</a> of the 1840s was caused by a plant disease that reached the Highlands in 1846, where 150,000 people faced disaster because their food supply was largely potatoes (with a little herring, oatmeal and milk). They were rescued by an effective emergency relief system that stands in dramatic contrast to the failures of relief in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rural_life">Rural life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Rural life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The unequal concentration of land ownership remained an emotional subject and eventually became a cornerstone of liberal radicalism. The politically powerless poor crofters embraced the popularly oriented, fervently evangelical Presbyterian <a href="/wiki/Christian_revival" title="Christian revival">revival</a> after 1800,<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> and the breakaway "Free Church" after 1843. This evangelical movement was led by lay preachers who themselves came from the lower strata, and whose preaching was implicitly critical of the established order. This energised the crofters and separated them from the landlords, preparing them for their successful and violent challenge to the landlords in the 1880s through the <a href="/wiki/Highland_Land_League" title="Highland Land League">Highland Land League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Violence began on the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Skye" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Skye">Isle of Skye</a> when Highland landlords cleared their lands for sheep and deer parks. It was quieted when the government stepped in passing the <a href="/wiki/Crofters%27_Holdings_(Scotland)_Act,_1886" class="mw-redirect" title="Crofters&#39; Holdings (Scotland) Act, 1886">Crofters\' Holdings (Scotland) Act, 1886</a> to reduce rents, guarantee fixity of tenure, and break up large estates to provide crofts for the homeless.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In 1885, three Independent Crofter candidates were elected to Parliament, leading to explicit security for the Scottish smallholders; the legal right to bequeath tenancies to descendants; and creating a <a href="/wiki/Crofting_Commission" title="Crofting Commission">Crofting Commission</a>. The Crofters as a political movement faded away by 1892, and the Liberal Party gained most of their votes.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue,_Dunfermline.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg/220px-Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg/330px-Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg/440px-Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue%2C_Dunfermline.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andrew_Carnegie%27s_statue,_Dunfermline.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Statue of emigrant, industrialist and philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a> in his home town of <a href="/wiki/Dunfermline" title="Dunfermline">Dunfermline</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The population of Scotland grew steadily in the 19th century, from 1,608,000 in the census of 1801 to 2,889,000 in 1851 and 4,472,000 in 1901.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Even with the development of industry there were insufficient good jobs; as a result, during the period 1841\xe2\x80\x931931, about 2 million Scots migrated to North America and Australia, and another 750,000 Scots relocated to England.<sup id="cite_ref-Huston&amp;Knox2001pxxxii_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huston&amp;Knox2001pxxxii-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> Scotland lost a much higher proportion of its population than England and Wales,<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson1990pp9-10_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1990pp9-10-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> reaching perhaps as much as 30.2 per cent of its <a href="/wiki/Natural_increase" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural increase">natural increase</a> from the 1850s onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> This not only limited Scotland\'s population increase, but meant that almost every family lost members due to emigration and, because more of the migrants were young males, it skewed the sex and age ratios of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson1990pp9-10_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1990pp9-10-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nScots-born migrants that played a leading role in the foundation and development of the United States included cleric and revolutionary <a href="/wiki/John_Witherspoon" title="John Witherspoon">John Witherspoon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> sailor <a href="/wiki/John_Paul_Jones" title="John Paul Jones">John Paul Jones</a>, industrialist and philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Andrew Carnegie</a>, and scientist and inventor <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Graham_Bell" title="Alexander Graham Bell">Alexander Graham Bell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In Canada they included soldier and governor of Quebec <a href="/wiki/James_Murray_(Quebec_governor)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Murray (Quebec governor)">James Murray</a>, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/John_A._Macdonald" title="John A. Macdonald">John A. Macdonald</a> and politician and social reformer <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Douglas" title="Tommy Douglas">Tommy Douglas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> For Australia they included soldier and governor <a href="/wiki/Lachlan_Macquarie" title="Lachlan Macquarie">Lachlan Macquarie</a>, governor and scientist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Brisbane" title="Thomas Brisbane">Thomas Brisbane</a> and Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Fisher" title="Andrew Fisher">Andrew Fisher</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> For New Zealand they included politician <a href="/wiki/Peter_Fraser" title="Peter Fraser">Peter Fraser</a> and outlaw <a href="/wiki/James_Mckenzie_(outlaw)" title="James Mckenzie (outlaw)">James Mckenzie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> By the 21st century, there would be about as many people who were <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Canadians" title="Scottish Canadians">Scottish Canadians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish American">Scottish Americans</a> as the 5 million remaining in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Huston&amp;Knox2001pxxxii_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huston&amp;Knox2001pxxxii-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_schism_and_revival">Religious schism and revival</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Religious schism and revival">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_Scotland" title="History of Christianity in Scotland">History of Christianity in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Chalmers_statue,_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg/170px-Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg/255px-Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg/340px-Thomas_Chalmers_statue%2C_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="2560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Chalmers_statue,_George_Street_Edinburgh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chalmers" title="Thomas Chalmers">Thomas Chalmers</a> statue in George Street, Edinburgh</div></div></div>\n<p>After prolonged years of struggle, in 1834 the Evangelicals gained control of the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">General Assembly</a> and passed the Veto Act, which allowed congregations to reject unwanted "intrusive" presentations to livings by patrons. The following "Ten Years\' Conflict" of legal and political wrangling ended in defeat for the non-intrusionists in the civil courts. The result was a schism from the church by some of the non-intrusionists led by Dr <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Chalmers" title="Thomas Chalmers">Thomas Chalmers</a> known as the Great <a href="/wiki/Disruption_of_1843" title="Disruption of 1843">Disruption of 1843</a>. Roughly a third of the clergy, mainly from the North and Highlands, formed the separate <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(1843%E2%80%931900)" title="Free Church of Scotland (1843\xe2\x80\x931900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>. The evangelical Free Churches, which were more accepting of Gaelic language and culture, grew rapidly in the Highlands and Islands, appealing much more strongly than did the established church.<sup id="cite_ref-Robb1990_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robb1990-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> Chalmers\'s ideas shaped the breakaway group. He stressed a social vision that revived and preserved Scotland\'s communal traditions at a time of strain on the social fabric of the country. Chalmers\'s idealised small equalitarian, kirk-based, self-contained communities that recognised the individuality of their members and the need for co-operation.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> That vision also affected the mainstream Presbyterian churches, and by the 1870s it had been assimilated by the established Church of Scotland. Chalmers\'s ideals demonstrated that the church was concerned with the problems of urban society, and they represented a real attempt to overcome the social fragmentation that took place in industrial towns and cities.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 19th century the major debates were between fundamentalist Calvinists and theological liberals, who rejected a literal interpretation of the Bible. This resulted in a further split in the Free Church as the rigid Calvinists broke away to form the <a href="/wiki/Free_Presbyterian_Church_of_Scotland" title="Free Presbyterian Church of Scotland">Free Presbyterian Church</a> in 1893.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> There were, however, also moves towards reunion, beginning with the unification of some secessionist churches into the <a href="/wiki/United_Secession_Church" title="United Secession Church">United Secession Church</a> in 1820, which united with the Relief Church in 1847 to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Presbyterian_Church_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Presbyterian Church of Scotland">United Presbyterian Church</a>, which in turn joined with the Free Church in 1900 to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Free_Church_of_Scotland" title="United Free Church of Scotland">United Free Church of Scotland</a>. The removal of legislation on lay patronage would allow the majority of the Free Church to rejoin Church of Scotland in 1929. The schisms left small denominations including the <a href="/wiki/Free_Presbyterian_Church_of_Scotland" title="Free Presbyterian Church of Scotland">Free Presbyterians</a> and a remnant that had not merged in 1900 as the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(post_1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (post 1900)">Free Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Catholic_Emancipation" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic Emancipation">Catholic Emancipation</a> in 1829 and the influx of large numbers of Irish immigrants, particularly after the famine years of the late 1840s, principally to the growing lowland centres like Glasgow, led to a transformation in the fortunes of Catholicism. In 1878, despite opposition, a Roman Catholic ecclesiastical hierarchy was restored to the country, and Catholicism became a significant denomination within Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Episcopalianism also revived in the 19th century as the issue of succession receded, becoming established as the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_in_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopal Church in Scotland">Episcopal Church in Scotland</a> in 1804, as an autonomous organisation in communion with the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baptist_Union_of_Scotland" title="Baptist Union of Scotland">Baptist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Congregational_Union_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregational Union of Scotland">Congregationalist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methodist" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodist">Methodist</a> churches had appeared in Scotland in the 18th century, but did not begin significant growth until the 19th century,<sup id="cite_ref-Koch2006p416-7_192-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koch2006p416-7-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> partly because more radical and evangelical traditions already existed within the Church of Scotland and the free churches. From 1879 they were joined by the evangelical revivalism of the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>, which attempted to make major inroads in the growing urban centres.<sup id="cite_ref-Ditchfield1998p91_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ditchfield1998p91-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_state_education">Development of state education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Development of state education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_Scotland" title="History of education in Scotland">History of education in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg/220px-Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg/330px-Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg/440px-Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2790" data-file-height="1928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mearns_Street_Public_School_28Feb10.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Mearns Street Public School built for the <a href="/wiki/Greenock" title="Greenock">Greenock</a> Burgh School Board.</div></div></div>\n<p>Industrialisation, urbanisation and the Disruption of 1843 all undermined the tradition of parish schools. From 1830 the state began to fund buildings with grants, then from 1846 it was funding schools by direct sponsorship, and in 1872 Scotland moved to a system like that in England of state-sponsored largely free schools, run by local school boards.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine2001p91-100_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine2001p91-100-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Overall administration was in the hands of the Scotch (later Scottish) Education Department in London.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Education was now compulsory from five to thirteen and many new board schools were built. Larger urban school boards established "higher grade" (secondary) schools as a cheaper alternative to the burgh schools. The Scottish Education Department introduced a Leaving Certificate Examination in 1888 to set national standards for secondary education and in 1890 school fees were abolished, creating a state-funded national system of free basic education and common examinations.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the beginning of the 19th century, Scottish universities had no entrance exam, students typically entered at ages of 15 or 16, attended for as little as two years, chose which lectures to attend and could leave without qualifications. After two commissions of enquiry in 1826 and 1876 and reforming acts of parliament in 1858 and 1889, the curriculum and system of graduation were reformed to meet the needs of the emerging middle classes and the professions. Entrance examinations equivalent to the School Leaving Certificate were introduced and average ages of entry rose to 17 or 18. Standard patterns of graduation in the arts curriculum offered 3-year ordinary and 4-year honours degrees and separate science faculties were able to move away from the compulsory Latin, Greek and philosophy of the old MA curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003p224_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003p224-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The historic University of Glasgow became a leader in British higher education by providing the educational needs of youth from the urban and commercial classes, as well as the upper class. It prepared students for non-commercial careers in government, the law, medicine, education, and the ministry and a smaller group for careers in science and engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup> St Andrews pioneered the admission of women to Scottish universities, creating the Lady Licentiate in Arts (LLA), which proved highly popular. From 1892 Scottish universities could admit and graduate women and the numbers of women at Scottish universities steadily increased until the early 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Rayner-Canham2008_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rayner-Canham2008-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing">Fishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Fishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The years before the First World War were the golden age of the inshore fisheries. Landings reached new heights, and Scottish catches dominated Europe\'s herring trade,<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> accounting for a third of the British catch. High productivity came about thanks to the transition to more productive steam-powered boats, while the rest of Europe\'s fishing fleets were slower because they were still powered by sails.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_realignment">Political realignment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Political realignment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WinstonChurchill1916Army.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/WinstonChurchill1916Army.gif/170px-WinstonChurchill1916Army.gif" width="170" height="218" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/WinstonChurchill1916Army.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WinstonChurchill1916Army.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots_Fusiliers" title="Royal Scots Fusiliers">Royal Scots Fusiliers</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Western_Front_(World_War_I)" title="Western Front (World War I)">Western Front</a> in 1916</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Khaki_Election" class="mw-redirect" title="Khaki Election">Khaki Election</a> of 1900, nationalist concern with the <a href="/wiki/Boer_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer War">Boer War</a> meant that the Conservatives and their Liberal Unionist allies gained a majority of Scottish seats for the first time, although the Liberals regained their ascendancy in the next election.<sup id="cite_ref-Hearn2000p45_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn2000p45-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> The Unionists and Conservatives merged in 1912,<sup id="cite_ref-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5_207-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> usually known as the Conservatives in England and Wales, they adopted the name <a href="/wiki/Unionist_Party_(Scotland)" title="Unionist Party (Scotland)">Unionist Party</a> in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> Scots played a major part in the leadership of UK political parties producing a Conservative Prime Minister in <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Balfour" title="Arthur Balfour">Arthur Balfour</a> (1902\xe2\x80\x9305) and a Liberal one in <a href="/wiki/Henry_Campbell-Bannerman" title="Henry Campbell-Bannerman">Henry Campbell-Bannerman</a> (1905\xe2\x80\x9308).<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Various organisations, including the Independent Labour Party, joined to make the British <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> in 1906, with Keir Hardie as its first chairman.<sup id="cite_ref-Hearn2000p45_265-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn2000p45-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="First_World_War_.281914.E2.80.941918.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War_(1914\xe2\x80\x941918)">First World War (1914\xe2\x80\x941918)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: First World War (1914\xe2\x80\x941918)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom_during_World_War_I" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the United Kingdom during World War I">History of the United Kingdom during World War I</a></div>\n<p>Scotland played a major role in the British effort in the First World War.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> It especially provided manpower, ships, machinery, food (particularly fish) and money, engaging with the conflict with some enthusiasm.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>  Scotland\'s industries were directed at the war effort. For example, <a href="/wiki/Singer_Corporation" title="Singer Corporation">the Singer Clydebank sewing machine factory</a> received over 5000 government contracts, and made 303 million artillery shells, shell components, fuses, and aeroplane parts, as well as grenades, rifle parts, and 361,000 horseshoes. Its labour force of 14,000 was about 70 percent female at war\'s end.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With a population of 4.8 million in 1911, Scotland sent 690,000 men to the war, of whom 74,000 died in combat or from disease, and 150,000 were seriously wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> Scottish urban centres, with their poverty and unemployment, were favourite recruiting grounds of the regular British army, and Dundee, where the female-dominated jute industry limited male employment, had one of the highest proportion of reservists and serving soldiers than almost any other British city.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Concern for their families\' standard of living made men hesitate to enlist; voluntary enlistment rates went up after the government guaranteed a weekly stipend for life to the survivors of men who were killed or disabled.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> After the introduction of conscription from January 1916 every part of the country was affected. Occasionally Scottish troops made up large proportions of the active combatants, and suffered corresponding loses, as at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Loos" title="Battle of Loos">Battle of Loos</a>, where there were three full Scots divisions and other Scottish units.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_272-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Thus, although Scots were only 10 per cent of the British population, they made up 15 per cent of the national armed forces and eventually accounted for 20 per cent of the dead.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p49_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p49-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> Some areas, like the thinly populated Island of <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Harris" title="Lewis and Harris">Lewis and Harris</a>, suffered some of the highest proportional losses of any part of Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991_272-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lenman&amp;Mackie1991-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> Clydeside shipyards and the nearby engineering shops were the major centres of war industry in Scotland. In Glasgow, radical agitation led to industrial and political unrest that continued after the war ended.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> After the end of the war in June 1919 the German fleet interned in Scapa Flow was <a href="/wiki/Scuttling_of_the_German_fleet_in_Scapa_Flow" title="Scuttling of the German fleet in Scapa Flow">scuttled</a> by its German crews, to avoid its ships being taken over by the victorious allies.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_boom_and_stagnation">Economic boom and stagnation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Economic boom and stagnation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg/170px-Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg/255px-Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg/340px-Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="646" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beardmore_advertisement_Brasseys_1923.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1923 advert for <a href="/wiki/William_Beardmore_and_Company" title="William Beardmore and Company">William Beardmore and Company</a>, Clydeside, who employed 40,000 workers at its height</div></div></div>\n<p>A boom was created by the First World War, with the shipbuilding industry expanding by a third, but a serious depression hit the economy by 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> The most skilled craftsmen were especially hard hit, because there were few alternative uses for their specialised skills.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> The main social indicators such as poor health, bad housing, and long-term mass unemployment, pointed to terminal social and economic stagnation at best, or even a downward spiral. The heavy dependence on obsolescent heavy industry and mining was a central problem, and no one offered workable solutions. The despair reflected what Finlay (1994) describes as a widespread sense of hopelessness that prepared local business and political leaders to accept a new orthodoxy of centralised government economic planning when it arrived during the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A few industries did grow, such as chemicals and whisky, which developed a global market for premium "Scotch".<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> However, in general the Scottish economy stagnated leading to growing unemployment and political agitation among industrial workers.<sup id="cite_ref-Hearn2000p45_265-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hearn2000p45-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interwar_politics">Interwar politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Interwar politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After World War I the Liberal Party began to disintegrate and Labour emerged as the party of progressive politics in Scotland, gaining a solid following among working classes of the urban lowlands. As a result, the Unionists were able to gain most of the votes of the middle classes, who now feared <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik">Bolshevik</a> revolution, setting the social and geographical electoral pattern in Scotland that would last until the late 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5_207-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Devine&amp;Finlay1996pp64-5-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> The fear of the left had been fuelled by the emergence of a radical movement led by militant trades unionists. <a href="/wiki/John_Maclean_(Scottish_socialist)" title="John Maclean (Scottish socialist)">John MacLean</a> emerged as a key political figure in what became known as <a href="/wiki/Red_Clydeside" title="Red Clydeside">Red Clydeside</a>, and in January 1919, the British Government, fearful of a revolutionary uprising, deployed tanks and soldiers in central Glasgow. Formerly a Liberal stronghold, the industrial districts switched to Labour by 1922, with a base in the Irish Catholic working class districts. Women were especially active in building neighbourhood solidarity on housing and rent issues. However, the "Reds" operated within the Labour Party and had little influence in Parliament; in the face of heavy unemployment the workers\' mood changed to passive despair by the late 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> Scottish educated <a href="/wiki/Bonar_Law" title="Bonar Law">Bonar Law</a> led a Conservative government from 1922 to 1923<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> and another Scot, <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a>, would be the Labour Party\'s first Prime Minister in 1924 and again from 1929 to 1935.<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With all the main parties committed to the Union, new nationalist and independent political groupings began to emerge, including the <a href="/wiki/National_Party_of_Scotland" title="National Party of Scotland">National Party of Scotland</a> in 1928 and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Party" title="Scottish Party">Scottish Party</a> in 1930. They joined to form the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> (SNP) in 1934, with the goal of creating an <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">independent Scotland</a>, but it enjoyed little electoral success in the Westminster system.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Second_World_War_1939.E2.80.9345"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War_1939\xe2\x80\x9345">Second World War 1939\xe2\x80\x9345</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Second World War 1939\xe2\x80\x9345">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Home_front_during_World_War_II#Britain" title="Home front during World War II">Home front during World War II \xc2\xa7&#160;Britain</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg/220px-Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg" width="220" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg/330px-Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg/440px-Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Royal_Scots_with_flag_01-1945.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Royal_Scots" title="Royal Scots">Royal Scots</a> with captured Japanese flag, Burma, January 1945</div></div></div>\n<p>As in World War I, <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a> in <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> served as an important <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> base. Attacks on Scapa Flow and <a href="/wiki/Rosyth" title="Rosyth">Rosyth</a> gave RAF fighters their first successes downing bombers in the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Lothian" title="East Lothian">East Lothian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> The shipyards and heavy engineering factories in <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a> and Clydeside played a key part in the war effort, and suffered attacks from the <a href="/wiki/Luftwaffe" title="Luftwaffe">Luftwaffe</a>, enduring great destruction and loss of life.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p51_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p51-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> As transatlantic voyages involved negotiating north-west Britain, Scotland played a key part in the battle of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>\'s relative proximity to occupied <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Shetland_Bus" class="mw-redirect" title="Shetland Bus">Shetland Bus</a> by which fishing boats helped Norwegians flee the <a href="/wiki/Nazis" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazis">Nazis</a>, and expeditions across the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> to assist resistance.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Significant individual contributions to the war effort by Scots included the invention of radar by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Watson-Watt" title="Robert Watson-Watt">Robert Watson-Watt</a>, which was invaluable in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Britain" title="Battle of Britain">Battle of Britain</a>, as was the leadership at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fighter_Command" title="RAF Fighter Command">RAF Fighter Command</a> of Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dowding" title="Hugh Dowding">Hugh Dowding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In World War II, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a> appointed Labour politician <a href="/wiki/Tom_Johnston_(British_politician)" title="Tom Johnston (British politician)">Tom Johnston</a> as <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Scotland" title="Secretary of State for Scotland">Secretary of State for Scotland</a> in February 1941; he controlled Scottish affairs until the war ended. He launched numerous initiatives to promote Scotland, attracting businesses and new jobs through his new Scottish Council of Industry. He set up 32 committees to deal with social and economic problems, ranging from juvenile delinquency to sheep farming. He regulated rents, and set up a prototype national health service, using new hospitals set up in the expectation of large numbers of casualties from German bombing.
