His most successful venture was setting up a system of hydro electricity using water power in the Highlands.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> A long-standing supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule">Home Rule</a> movement, Johnston persuaded Churchill of the need to counter the nationalist threat north of the border and created a Scottish Council of State and a Council of Industry as institutions to <a href="/wiki/Devolved_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Devolved government">devolve</a> some power away from <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In World War II, despite extensive bombing by the Luftwaffe, Scottish industry came out of the depression slump by a dramatic expansion of its industrial activity, absorbing unemployed men and many women as well. The shipyards were the centre of more activity, but many smaller industries produced the machinery needed by the British bombers, tanks and warships.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p51_284-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p51-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> Agriculture prospered, as did all sectors except for coal mining, which was operating mines near exhaustion. Real wages, adjusted for inflation, rose 25 per cent, and unemployment temporarily vanished. Increased income, and the more equal distribution of food, obtained through a tight rationing system, dramatically improved the health and nutrition; the average height of 13-year-olds in Glasgow increased by 2 inches (51&#160;mm).<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mass_migration">End of mass migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: End of mass migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While emigration began to tail off in England and Wales after the First World War,<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson1990pp9-10_249-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1990pp9-10-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> it continued apace in Scotland, with 400,000 Scots, ten per cent of the population, estimated to have left the country between 1921 and 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan2003p51_284-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan2003p51-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The economic stagnation was only one factor; other push factors included a zest for travel and adventure, and the pull factors of better job opportunities abroad, personal networks to link into, and the basic cultural similarity of the United States, Canada, and Australia. Government subsidies for travel and relocation facilitated the decision to emigrate. Personal networks of family and friends who had gone ahead and wrote back, or sent money, prompted emigrants to retrace their paths.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> hit in the 1930s there were no easily available jobs in the US and Canada and emigration fell to less than 50,000 a year, bringing to an end the period of mass migrations that had opened in the mid-18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_renaissance">Literary renaissance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Literary renaissance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg/170px-Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg/255px-Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg/340px-Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="516" data-file-height="676" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lamb-Hugh_MacDiarmid.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of <a href="/wiki/Hugh_MacDiarmid" title="Hugh MacDiarmid">Hugh MacDiarmid</a> sculpted by <a href="/wiki/William_Lamb_(sculptor)" title="William Lamb (sculptor)">William Lamb</a> in 1927</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Renaissance" title="Scottish Renaissance">Scottish Renaissance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature of Scotland">Literature of Scotland</a></div>\n<p>In the early 20th century there was a new surge of activity in Scottish literature, influenced by <a href="/wiki/Modernism" title="Modernism">modernism</a> and resurgent nationalism, known as the Scottish Renaissance.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> The leading figure in the movement was <a href="/wiki/Hugh_MacDiarmid" title="Hugh MacDiarmid">Hugh MacDiarmid</a> (the pseudonym of Christopher Murray Grieve). MacDiarmid attempted to revive the Scots language as a medium for serious literature in poetic works including "<a href="/wiki/A_Drunk_Man_Looks_at_the_Thistle" title="A Drunk Man Looks at the Thistle">A Drunk Man Looks at the Thistle</a>" (1936), developing a form of <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="Synthetic Scots">Synthetic Scots</a> that combined different regional dialects and archaic terms.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Other writers that emerged in this period, and are often treated as part of the movement, include the poets <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Muir" title="Edwin Muir">Edwin Muir</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Soutar" title="William Soutar">William Soutar</a>, the novelists <a href="/wiki/Neil_Gunn" class="mw-redirect" title="Neil Gunn">Neil Gunn</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Blake" title="George Blake">George Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nan_Shepherd" title="Nan Shepherd">Nan Shepherd</a>, <a href="/wiki/A._J._Cronin" title="A. J. Cronin">A. J. Cronin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naomi_Mitchison" title="Naomi Mitchison">Naomi Mitchison</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Linklater" title="Eric Linklater">Eric Linklater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Grassic_Gibbon" title="Lewis Grassic Gibbon">Lewis Grassic Gibbon</a>, and the playwright <a href="/wiki/James_Bridie" title="James Bridie">James Bridie</a>. All were born within a fifteen-year period (1887 and 1901) and, although they cannot be described as members of a single school, they all pursued an exploration of identity, rejecting nostalgia and parochialism and engaging with social and political issues.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_reorganisation_and_retrenchment">Educational reorganisation and retrenchment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Educational reorganisation and retrenchment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_Scotland" title="History of education in Scotland">History of education in Scotland</a></div>\n<p>In the 20th century, the centre of the education system became more focused on Scotland, with the ministry of education partly moving north in 1918 and then finally having its headquarters relocated to Edinburgh in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The school leaving age was raised to 14 in 1901, but despite attempts to raise it to 15 this was only made law in 1939 and then postponed because of the outbreak of war. In 1918, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> schools were brought into the state system, but retained their distinct religious character, access to schools by priests and the requirement that school staff be acceptable to the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first half of the 20th century saw Scottish universities fall behind those in England and Europe in terms of participation and investment. The decline of traditional industries between the wars undermined recruitment. English universities increased the numbers of students registered between 1924 and 1927 by 19 per cent, but in Scotland the numbers fell, particularly among women. In the same period, while expenditure in English universities rose by 90 per cent, in Scotland the increase was less than a third of that figure.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_role">Naval role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Naval role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMNB_Clyde.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/170px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/255px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/340px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMNB_Clyde.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/HMNB_Clyde" title="HMNB Clyde">HMNB Clyde</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Scotland\'s <a href="/wiki/Scapa_Flow" title="Scapa Flow">Scapa Flow</a> was the main base for the Royal Navy in the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> intensified in 1961, the United States deployed <a href="/wiki/Polaris_ballistic_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris ballistic missile">Polaris ballistic missiles</a>, and submarines, in the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Holy_Loch" title="Holy Loch">Holy Loch</a>. Public protests from <a href="/wiki/CND" class="mw-redirect" title="CND">CND</a> campaigners proved futile. The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> successfully convinced the government to allow the base because it wanted its own <i>Polaris</i>-class submarines, and it obtained them in 1963. The RN\'s nuclear submarine base opened four <a href="/wiki/Resolution_class_submarine" class="mw-redirect" title="Resolution class submarine"><i>Resolution</i> class</a> <a href="/wiki/Polaris_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris missile">Polaris</a> submarines at the expanded <a href="/wiki/HMNB_Clyde" title="HMNB Clyde">Faslane Naval Base</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Gare_Loch" title="Gare Loch">Gare Loch</a>. The first patrol of a <a href="/wiki/Trident_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident missile">Trident</a>-armed submarine occurred in 1994, although the US base was closed at the end of the Cold War.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Postwar">Postwar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Postwar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Social history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)" title="Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)">Political history of the United Kingdom (1945\xe2\x80\x93present)</a></div>\n<p>After World War II, Scotland\'s economic situation became progressively worse due to overseas competition, inefficient industry, and industrial disputes. This only began to change in the 1970s, partly due to the discovery and development of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> oil and gas and partly as Scotland moved towards a more service-based economy. This period saw the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> and movements for both <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a> and more popularly <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolution</a>. However, a referendum on devolution in 1979 was unsuccessful as it did not achieve the support of 40 per cent of the electorate (despite a small majority of those who voted supporting the proposal.)\n</p><p>A <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">national referendum</a> to decide on  <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence" title="Scottish independence">Scottish independence</a> was held on 18 September 2014. Voters were asked to answer either "Yes" or "No" to the question: "Should Scotland be an independent country?"<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> 55.3% of voters answered "No" and 44.7% answered "Yes", with a <a href="/wiki/Voter_turnout" title="Voter turnout">voter turnout</a> of 84.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-Now_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Now-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_devolution">Politics and devolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Politics and devolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Scotland" title="Politics of Scotland">Politics of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_Parliament_building,_Holyrood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg/220px-Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg/330px-Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg/440px-Scottish_Parliament_building%2C_Holyrood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scottish_Parliament_building,_Holyrood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_building" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Parliament building">Scottish Parliament building</a>, Holyrood, opened in 2004 and intended to evoke the crags of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_landscape" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish landscape">Scottish landscape</a> and, in places, upturned <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry_in_Scotland" title="Fishing industry in Scotland">fishing boats</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the second half of the 20th century the Labour Party usually won most Scottish seats in the Westminster parliament, losing this dominance briefly to the <a href="/wiki/Unionist_Party_(Scotland)" title="Unionist Party (Scotland)">Unionists</a> in the 1950s. Support in Scotland was critical to Labour\'s overall electoral fortunes as without Scottish MPs it would have gained only two UK electoral victories in the 20th century (1945 and 1966).<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> The number of Scottish seats represented by Unionists (known as <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservatives</a> from 1965 onwards) went into steady decline from 1959 onwards, until it fell to zero in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> Politicians with Scottish connections continued to play a prominent part in UK political life, with Prime Ministers including the Conservatives <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> (whose father was Scottish) from 1957 to 1963 and <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a> from 1963 to 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> gained its first seat at Westminster in 1945 and became a party of national prominence during the 1970s, achieving 11 MPs in 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93_282-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> However, a referendum on devolution in 1979 was unsuccessful as it did not achieve the necessary support of 40 per cent of the electorate (despite a small majority of those who voted supporting the proposal) and the SNP went into electoral decline during the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93_282-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke&amp;Stenevson2000p93-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> The introduction in 1989 by the <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Thatcher</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> government of the <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">Community Charge</a> (widely known as the Poll Tax), one year before the rest of the United Kingdom, contributed to a growing movement for a return to direct Scottish control over domestic affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> The electoral success of New Labour in 1997 was led by two Prime Ministers with Scottish connections: Tony Blair (who was brought up in Scotland) from 1997 to 2007 and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> from 2007 to 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-Quinault2007_211-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quinault2007-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> opened the way for constitutional change. On 11 September 1997, the 700th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Blair</a> led <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government again held a referendum on the issue of devolution. A positive outcome led to the establishment of a devolved <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> in 1999. A <a href="/wiki/Coalition" title="Coalition">coalition</a> government, which would last until 2007, was formed between Labour and the Liberal Democrats, with <a href="/wiki/Donald_Dewar" title="Donald Dewar">Donald Dewar</a> as <a href="/wiki/First_Minister_of_Scotland" title="First Minister of Scotland">First Minister</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> The new <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament_Building" title="Scottish Parliament Building">Scottish Parliament Building</a>, adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Holyrood_House" class="mw-redirect" title="Holyrood House">Holyrood House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, opened in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Audit_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audit-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Although not initially reaching its 1970s peak in Westminster elections, the SNP had more success in the Scottish Parliamentary elections with their system of <a href="/wiki/Mixed_member_proportional_representation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mixed member proportional representation">mixed member proportional representation</a>. It became the official opposition in 1999, a minority government in 2007 and a majority government from 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_independence_referendum,_2014" title="Scottish independence referendum, 2014">independence referendum</a> saw voters reject independence, choosing instead to remain in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> In the 2015 Westminster election, the SNP won 56 out of 59 Scottish seats, making them the third largest party in Westminster.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_reorientation">Economic reorientation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Economic reorientation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Scotland" title="Economy of Scotland">Economy of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg/220px-Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg/330px-Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg/440px-Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_platform_in_the_North_SeaPros.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Drilling_rig" title="Drilling rig">drilling rig</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a></div></div></div>\n<p>After World War II, Scotland\'s economic situation became progressively worse due to overseas competition, inefficient industry, and industrial disputes.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> This only began to change in the 1970s, partly due to the discovery and development of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> oil and gas and partly as Scotland moved towards a more service-based economy. The discovery of the giant <a href="/wiki/Forties_oilfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Forties oilfield">Forties oilfield</a> in October 1970 signalled that Scotland was about to become a major oil producing nation, a view confirmed when Shell Expro discovered the giant <a href="/wiki/Brent_oilfield" title="Brent oilfield">Brent oilfield</a> in the northern North Sea east of Shetland in 1971. Oil production started from the Argyll field (now Ardmore) in June 1975, followed by Forties in November of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> Deindustrialisation took place rapidly in the 1970s and 1980s, as most of the traditional industries drastically shrank or were completely closed down. A new service-oriented economy emerged to replace traditional heavy industries.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> This included a resurgent financial services industry and the <a href="/wiki/Electronics_manufacturing" class="mw-redirect" title="Electronics manufacturing">electronics manufacturing</a> of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Glen" title="Silicon Glen">Silicon Glen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_diversity_and_decline">Religious diversity and decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Religious diversity and decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Scotland" title="Religion in Scotland">Religion in Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg/220px-Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg/330px-Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg/440px-Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfm_glasgow_central_mosque_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Central_Mosque" title="Glasgow Central Mosque">Glasgow Central Mosque</a>, the largest <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a> in Scotland</div></div></div><p>\nIn the 20th century existing Christian denominations were joined by other organisations, including the <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Brethren" title="Plymouth Brethren">Brethren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pentecostal" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentecostal">Pentecostal</a> churches. Although some denominations thrived, after World War II there was a steady overall decline in church attendance and resulting church closures for most denominations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ditchfield1998p91_193-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ditchfield1998p91-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> Talks began in the 1950s aiming at a grand merger of the main Presbyterian, Episcopal and Methodist bodies in Scotland. The talks were ended in 2003,  when the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_of_the_Church_of_Scotland" title="General Assembly of the Church of Scotland">General Assembly of the Church of Scotland</a> rejected the proposals.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 census</a>, 53.8% of the Scottish population identified as Christian (declining from 65.1% in 2001). The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> is the largest religious grouping in Scotland, with 32.4% of the population. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church_in_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church in Scotland">Roman Catholic Church</a> accounted for 15.9% of the population and is especially important in <a href="/wiki/West_Central_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="West Central Scotland">West Central Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a>. In recent years other religions have established a presence in Scotland, mainly through <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration to the United Kingdom">immigration</a> and higher birth rates among ethnic minorities, with a small number of <a href="/wiki/Religious_conversion" title="Religious conversion">converts</a>. Those with the most adherents in the 2011 census are <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Scotland" title="Islam in Scotland">Islam</a> (1.4%, mainly among immigrants from South Asia), <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Scotland" title="Hinduism in Scotland">Hinduism</a> (0.3%), <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Scotland" title="Buddhism in Scotland">Buddhism</a> (0.2%) and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism_in_Scotland" title="Sikhism in Scotland">Sikhism</a> (0.2%). Other minority faiths include the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith_in_Scotland" title="Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad Faith in Scotland">Bah\xc3\xa1\'\xc3\xad Faith</a> and small <a href="/wiki/Neopaganism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neopaganism">Neopagan</a> groups. There are also various organisations which actively promote <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularism</a>, included within the 43.6% who either indicated no religion or did not state a religion in the 2011 census.<sup id="cite_ref-census2011detail_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census2011detail-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_reforms">Educational reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Educational reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish education">Scottish education</a></div>\n<p>Although plans to raise the school leaving age to 15 in the 1940s were never ratified, increasing numbers stayed on beyond elementary education and it was eventually raised to 16 in 1973. As a result, secondary education was the major area of growth in the second half of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> New qualifications were developed to cope with changing aspirations and economics, with the Leaving Certificate being replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Certificate_of_Education" title="Scottish Certificate of Education">Scottish Certificate of Education</a> <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_Grade" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinary Grade">Ordinary Grade</a> (\'O-Grade\') and <a href="/wiki/Higher_Grade" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher Grade">Higher Grade</a> (\'Higher\') qualifications in 1962, which became the basic entry qualification for university study.<sup id="cite_ref-Anderson2003_203-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2003-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The higher education sector expanded in the second half of the 20th century, with four institutions being given university status in the 1960s (<a href="/wiki/University_of_Dundee" title="University of Dundee">Dundee</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heriot-Watt_University" title="Heriot-Watt University">Heriot-Watt</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Stirling" title="University of Stirling">Stirling</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_of_Strathclyde" title="University of Strathclyde">Strathclyde</a>) and five in the 1990s (<a href="/wiki/University_of_Abertay" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Abertay">Abertay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glasgow_Caledonian_University" title="Glasgow Caledonian University">Glasgow Caledonian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Napier_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Napier University">Napier</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paisley" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Paisley">Paisley</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Robert_Gordon_University" class="mw-redirect" title="The Robert Gordon University">Robert Gordon</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> After devolution, in 1999 the new <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Executive" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Executive">Scottish Executive</a> set up an Education Department and an <a href="/wiki/Enterprise,_Transport_and_Lifelong_Learning_Department_(Scotland)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enterprise, Transport and Lifelong Learning Department (Scotland)">Enterprise, Transport and Lifelong Learning Department</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fairley2003_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fairley2003-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> One of the major diversions from practice in England, possible because of devolution, was the abolition of <a href="/wiki/Tuition_fees_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Tuition fees in the United Kingdom">student tuition fees</a> in 1999, instead retaining a system of means-tested student grants.<sup id="cite_ref-Cauldwell2003_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cauldwell2003-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_literature">New literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: New literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature of Scotland">Literature of Scotland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carol_Ann_Duffy_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="170" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Carol_Ann_Duffy_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="564" data-file-height="730" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carol_Ann_Duffy_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carol_Ann_Duffy" title="Carol Ann Duffy">Carol Ann Duffy</a> the first Scottish <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate" class="mw-redirect" title="Poet Laureate">Poet Laureate</a></div></div></div><p>\nSome writers that emerged after the Second World War followed Hugh MacDiarmid by writing in Scots, including <a href="/wiki/Robert_Garioch" title="Robert Garioch">Robert Garioch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sydney_Goodsir_Smith" title="Sydney Goodsir Smith">Sydney Goodsir Smith</a>. Others demonstrated a greater interest in English language poetry, among them <a href="/wiki/Norman_MacCaig" title="Norman MacCaig">Norman MacCaig</a>, George Bruce and <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Lindsay" title="Maurice Lindsay">Maurice Lindsay</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_Mackay_Brown" title="George Mackay Brown">George Mackay Brown</a> from Orkney, and <a href="/wiki/Iain_Crichton_Smith" title="Iain Crichton Smith">Iain Crichton Smith</a> from Lewis, wrote both poetry and prose fiction shaped by their distinctive island backgrounds.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> The Glaswegian poet <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Morgan_(poet)" title="Edwin Morgan (poet)">Edwin Morgan</a> became known for translations of works from a wide range of European languages. He was also the first <a href="/wiki/Scots_Makar" class="mw-redirect" title="Scots Makar">Scots Makar</a> (the official <a href="/wiki/Poet_laureate" title="Poet laureate">national poet</a>), appointed by the inaugural Scottish government in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> Many major Scottish post-war novelists, such as <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Spark" title="Muriel Spark">Muriel Spark</a>, with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prime_of_Miss_Jean_Brodie_(novel)" title="The Prime of Miss Jean Brodie (novel)">The Prime of Miss Jean Brodie</a></i> (1961) spent much or most of their lives outside Scotland, but often dealt with Scottish themes.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Successful mass-market works included the action novels of <a href="/wiki/Alistair_MacLean" title="Alistair MacLean">Alistair MacLean</a>, and the historical fiction of <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Dunnett" title="Dorothy Dunnett">Dorothy Dunnett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> A younger generation of novelists that emerged in the 1960s and 1970s included <a href="/wiki/Shena_Mackay" title="Shena Mackay">Shena Mackay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Spence" title="Alan Spence">Alan Spence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allan_Massie" title="Allan Massie">Allan Massie</a> and the work of <a href="/wiki/William_McIlvanney" title="William McIlvanney">William McIlvanney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> From the 1980s Scottish literature enjoyed another major revival, particularly associated with a group of Glasgow writers focused around critic, poet and teacher <a href="/wiki/Philip_Hobsbaum" title="Philip Hobsbaum">Philip Hobsbaum</a> and editor <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kravitz" title="Peter Kravitz">Peter Kravitz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> In the 1990s major, prize winning, Scottish novels, often overtly political, that emerged from this movement included <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Welsh" title="Irvine Welsh">Irvine Welsh</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Trainspotting_(novel)" title="Trainspotting (novel)">Trainspotting</a></i> (1993), Warner\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Morvern_Callar" title="Morvern Callar">Morvern Callar</a></i> (1995), Gray\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Poor_Things" title="Poor Things">Poor Things</a></i> (1992) and Kelman\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/How_Late_It_Was,_How_Late" title="How Late It Was, How Late">How Late It Was, How Late</a></i> (1994).<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> Scottish crime fiction has been a major area of growth, particularly the success of Edinburgh\'s <a href="/wiki/Ian_Rankin" title="Ian Rankin">Ian Rankin</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Inspector_Rebus" title="Inspector Rebus">Inspector Rebus</a> novels.<sup id="cite_ref-VisitingArtsScotland_293-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VisitingArtsScotland-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> This period also saw the emergence of a new generation of Scottish poets that became leading figures on the UK stage, including <a href="/wiki/Carol_Ann_Duffy" title="Carol Ann Duffy">Carol Ann Duffy</a>, who was named as <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate" class="mw-redirect" title="Poet Laureate">Poet Laureate</a> in May 2009, the first woman, the first Scot and the first openly gay poet to take the post.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_Scotland" title="Historiography of Scotland">Historiography of Scotland</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Scotland&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">\n<li style="display:table-row"><span United Kingdom</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of Scotland">Kings of Scotland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_years_in_Scotland" title="List of years in Scotland">List of years in Scotland</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Scottish_clan" title="Scottish clan">Scottish clan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Scottish_history" title="Timeline of Scottish history">Timeline of Scottish history</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
